For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Milky_Way_(disambiguation)" title="Milky Way (disambiguation)">Milky Way (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">spiral galaxy containing our Solar System</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<table class="infobox" cm<sup>-3</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kafle2014_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kafle2014-9">[9]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background: #30D5C8;">See also: <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">Galaxy</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_galaxies" title="List of galaxies">List of galaxies</a></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>Milky Way</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxy</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-context_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-context-17">[nb 1]</a></sup> that contains our <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>. The name describes the galaxy\'s appearance from <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>: a hazy band of light seen in the <a href="/wiki/Night_sky" title="Night sky">night sky</a> formed from stars that cannot be individually distinguished by the <a href="/wiki/Naked_eye" title="Naked eye">naked eye</a>. The term <i>Milky Way</i> is a translation of the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">via lactea</i></i>, from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#947;&#945;&#955;&#945;&#958;&#943;&#945;&#962; &#954;&#973;&#954;&#955;&#959;&#962;</span> (<i><i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek-language transliteration">galax&#237;as k&#253;klos</i></i>, "milky circle").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eo_galaxy_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-eo_galaxy-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jankowski_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jankowski-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schiller_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schiller-20">[19]</a></sup> From Earth, the Milky Way appears as a band because its disk-shaped structure is viewed from within. <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a> first resolved the band of light into individual stars with his telescope in 1610. Until the early 1920s, most astronomers thought that the Milky Way contained all the stars in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">Universe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> Following the 1920 <a href="/wiki/Great_Debate_(astronomy)" title="Great Debate (astronomy)">Great Debate</a> between the astronomers <a href="/wiki/Harlow_Shapley" title="Harlow Shapley">Harlow Shapley</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heber_Curtis" title="Heber Curtis">Heber Curtis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapley_curtis_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-shapley_curtis-22">[21]</a></sup> observations by <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Hubble" title="Edwin Hubble">Edwin Hubble</a> showed that the Milky Way is just one of many galaxies.\nThe Milky Way is a <a href="/wiki/Barred_spiral_galaxy" title="Barred spiral galaxy">barred spiral galaxy</a> with a diameter between 150,000 and 200,000 <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-years</a> (ly).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rpi2015_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-rpi2015-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup> It is estimated to contain 100&#8211;400 billion <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup> and more than 100 billion <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planets" title="Planets">planets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature-20120111_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nature-20120111-29">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space-20130102_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Space-20130102-30">[29]</a></sup> The Solar System is located at a radius of 26,490 (&#177; 100) light-years from the <a href="/wiki/Galactic_Center" title="Galactic Center">Galactic Center</a>, on the inner edge of the <a href="/wiki/Orion_Arm" title="Orion Arm">Orion Arm</a>, one of the spiral-shaped concentrations of gas and dust. The stars in the innermost 10,000 light-years form a <a href="/wiki/Bulge_(astronomy)" title="Bulge (astronomy)">bulge</a> and one or more bars that radiate from the bulge. The galactic center is an intense radio source known as <a href="/wiki/Sagittarius_A*" title="Sagittarius A*">Sagittarius A*</a>, assumed to be a <a href="/wiki/Supermassive_black_hole" title="Supermassive black hole">supermassive black hole</a> of 4.100 (&#177; 0.034) million <a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass">solar masses</a>.\n</p><p>Stars and gases at a wide range of distances from the Galactic Center orbit at approximately 220 kilometers per second. The constant rotation speed contradicts the laws of <a href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_laws_of_planetary_motion" title="Kepler\'s laws of planetary motion">Keplerian dynamics</a> and suggests that much of the <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> of the Milky Way is invisible to our telescopes, neither emitting nor absorbing <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a>. This conjectural mass has been termed "<a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koupelis_Kuhn2007_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koupelis_Kuhn2007-31">[30]</a></sup> The rotational period is about 240 million years at the radius of the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sparke_gallagher_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-sparke_gallagher-14">[14]</a></sup> The Milky Way as a whole is moving at a velocity of approximately 600&#160;km per second with respect to extragalactic frames of reference. The oldest stars in the Milky Way are nearly as old as the Universe itself and thus probably formed shortly after the <a href="/wiki/Chronology_of_the_universe#Dark_Ages" title="Chronology of the universe">Dark Ages</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HD_140283_(arXiv)_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-HD_140283_(arXiv)-32">[31]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Milky Way has several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Milky_Way%27s_satellite_galaxies" title="List of Milky Way\'s satellite galaxies">satellite galaxies</a> and is part of the <a href="/wiki/Local_Group" title="Local Group">Local Group</a> of galaxies, which form part of the <a href="/wiki/Virgo_Supercluster" title="Virgo Supercluster">Virgo Supercluster</a>, which is itself a component of the <a href="/wiki/Laniakea_Supercluster" title="Laniakea Supercluster">Laniakea Supercluster</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Appearance"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span history</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Environment"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Environment</span></a></li>\n<li class="tocnumber">9.1</span> <span class="toctext">Mapping</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Appearance">Appearance</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Milky_Way_Night_Sky_Black_Rock_Desert_Nevada.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4078" data-file-width="2912" decoding="async" codecs=&amp;quot;vp9, opus&amp;quot;" data-title="VP9 (360P)" data-shorttitle="VP9 360P" data-transcodekey="360p.vp9.webm" data-width="640" data-height="360" data-bandwidth="347216" data-framerate="25"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/d/d0/Following_the_Milky_Way_over_ALMA.webm/Following_the_Milky_Way_over_ALMA.webm.360p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="WebM (360P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 360P" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="640" data-height="360" data-bandwidth="505040" data-framerate="25"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;\'><img alt="File:Following the Milky Way over ALMA.webm" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Following_the_Milky_Way_over_ALMA.webm/220px--Following_the_Milky_Way_over_ALMA.webm.jpg" style="width:220px;height:124px"/><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d0/Following_the_Milky_Way_over_ALMA.webm" target="new" title="Play media"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Following_the_Milky_Way_over_ALMA.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Time-lapse" title="Time-lapse">time-lapse</a> video captures the Milky Way arching over <a href="/wiki/Atacama_Large_Millimeter_Array" title="Atacama Large Millimeter Array">ALMA</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Milky Way is visible from Earth as a hazy band of white light, some 30&#176; wide, arching across the <a href="/wiki/Night_sky" title="Night sky">night sky</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pasachoff1994_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-pasachoff1994-35">[34]</a></sup> In night sky observing, although all the individual naked-eye <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> in the entire sky are part of the Milky Way, the term &#8220;Milky Way&#8221; is limited to this band of light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup> The light originates from the accumulation of <a href="/wiki/Angular_resolution" title="Angular resolution">unresolved</a> stars and other material located in the direction of the <a href="/wiki/Galactic_plane" title="Galactic plane">galactic plane</a>. Dark regions within the band, such as the <a href="/wiki/Great_Rift_(astronomy)" title="Great Rift (astronomy)">Great Rift</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Coalsack_Nebula" title="Coalsack Nebula">Coalsack</a>, are areas where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstellar_dust" title="Interstellar dust">interstellar dust</a> blocks light from distant stars. The area of sky that the Milky Way obscures is called the <a href="/wiki/Zone_of_Avoidance" title="Zone of Avoidance">Zone of Avoidance</a>.\n</p><p>The Milky Way has a relatively low <a href="/wiki/Surface_brightness" title="Surface brightness">surface brightness</a>. Its visibility can be greatly reduced by background light, such as <a href="/wiki/Light_pollution" title="Light pollution">light pollution</a> or <a href="/wiki/Moonlight" title="Moonlight">moonlight</a>. The sky needs to be darker than about 20.2 <a href="/wiki/Magnitude_(astronomy)" title="Magnitude (astronomy)">magnitude</a> per square arcsecond in order for the Milky Way to be visible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> It should be visible if the <a href="/wiki/Limiting_magnitude" title="Limiting magnitude">limiting magnitude</a> is approximately +5.1 or better and shows a great deal of detail at +6.1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-steinicke_sakiel2007_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-steinicke_sakiel2007-39">[38]</a></sup> This makes the Milky Way difficult to see from brightly lit urban or suburban areas, but very prominent when viewed from <a href="/wiki/Rural_area" title="Rural area">rural areas</a> when the Moon is below the horizon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bortle_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-bortle-40">[nb 2]</a></sup> Maps of artificial night sky brightness show that more than one-third of Earth\'s population cannot see the Milky Way from their homes due to light pollution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[39]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As viewed from Earth, the visible region of the Milky Way\'s <a href="/wiki/Galactic_plane" title="Galactic plane">galactic plane</a> occupies an area of the sky that includes 30 <a href="/wiki/Constellation" title="Constellation">constellations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[40]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Galactic_Center" title="Galactic Center">Galactic Center</a> lies in the direction of <a href="/wiki/Sagittarius_(constellation)" title="Sagittarius (constellation)">Sagittarius</a>, where the Milky Way is brightest. From Sagittarius, the hazy band of white light appears to pass around to the <a href="/wiki/Galactic_anticenter" title="Galactic anticenter">galactic anticenter</a> in <a href="/wiki/Auriga_(constellation)" title="Auriga (constellation)">Auriga</a>. The band then continues the rest of the way around the sky, back to Sagittarius, dividing the sky into two roughly equal <a href="/wiki/Celestial_sphere" title="Celestial sphere">hemispheres</a>.\n</p><p>The galactic plane is inclined by about 60&#176; to the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a> (the plane of <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_orbit" title="Earth\'s orbit">Earth\'s orbit</a>). Relative to the <a href="/wiki/Celestial_equator" title="Celestial equator">celestial equator</a>, it passes as far north as the constellation of <a href="/wiki/Cassiopeia_(constellation)" title="Cassiopeia (constellation)">Cassiopeia</a> and as far south as the constellation of <a href="/wiki/Crux" title="Crux">Crux</a>, indicating the high inclination of Earth\'s <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equatorial plane</a> and the plane of the ecliptic, relative to the galactic plane. The north galactic pole is situated at <a href="/wiki/Right_ascension" title="Right ascension">right ascension</a> 12<sup>h</sup> 49<sup>m</sup>, <a href="/wiki/Declination" title="Declination">declination</a> +27.4&#176; (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B1950" title="B1950">B1950</a>) near <a href="/wiki/Beta_Comae_Berenices" title="Beta Comae Berenices">&#946; Comae Berenices</a>, and the south galactic pole is near <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Sculptoris" title="Alpha Sculptoris">&#945; Sculptoris</a>. Because of this high inclination, depending on the time of night and year, the arch of the Milky Way may appear relatively low or relatively high in the sky. For observers from latitudes approximately 65&#176; north to 65&#176; south, the Milky Way passes <a href="/wiki/Zenith" title="Zenith">directly overhead</a> twice a day.\n</p>\n<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:602px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Milky_Way_Arch.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1212" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Milky_Way_Arch.jpg/600px-Milky_Way_Arch.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Milky_Way_Arch.jpg/900px-Milky_Way_Arch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Milky_Way_Arch.jpg/1200px-Milky_Way_Arch.jpg 2x" width="600"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Milky_Way_Arch.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Milky Way arching at a high inclination across the <a href="/wiki/Night_sky" title="Night sky">night sky</a>. (This <a href="/wiki/Compositing" title="Compositing">composited</a> <a href="/wiki/Panorama" title="Panorama">panorama</a> was taken at <a href="/wiki/Paranal_Observatory" title="Paranal Observatory">Paranal Observatory</a> in northern Chile). The bright object is Jupiter in the constellation <a href="/wiki/Sagittarius_(constellation)" title="Sagittarius (constellation)">Sagittarius</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Magellanic_Clouds" title="Magellanic Clouds">Magellanic Clouds</a> can be seen on the left. Galactic north is downward.</div></div></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Size_and_mass">Size and mass</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:UGC_12158.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="2790" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/UGC_12158.jpg/250px-UGC_12158.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/UGC_12158.jpg/375px-UGC_12158.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/UGC_12158.jpg/500px-UGC_12158.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:UGC_12158.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">HST</a> image of galaxy <a href="/wiki/UGC_12158" title="UGC 12158">UGC 12158</a>, which is thought to resemble the Milky Way in appearance</div></div></div>\n<p>The Milky Way is the second-largest galaxy in the <a href="/wiki/Local_Group" title="Local Group">Local Group</a>, with its stellar disk approximately 100,000&#160;ly (30&#160;kpc) in diameter and, on average, approximately 1,000&#160;ly (0.3&#160;kpc) thick.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ask-astro_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-ask-astro-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rix_Bovy_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rix_Bovy-4">[4]</a></sup> To compare the relative physical scale of the Milky Way, if the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> out to <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a> were the size of a <a href="/wiki/Quarter_(United_States_coin)" title="Quarter (United States coin)">US quarter</a> (24.3&#160;mm (0.955&#160;in)), the Milky Way would be approximately the size of the <a href="/wiki/Contiguous_United_States" title="Contiguous United States">contiguous United States</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[41]</a></sup> There is a ring-like filament of stars rippling above and below the relatively flat <a href="/wiki/Galactic_plane" title="Galactic plane">galactic plane</a>, wrapping around the Milky Way at a diameter of 150,000&#8211;180,000 light-years (46&#8211;55&#160;kpc),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[42]</a></sup> which may be part of the Milky Way itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rpi2015_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-rpi2015-25">[24]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Milky_way_profile.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1650" data-file-width="1576" decoding="async" height="262" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Milky_way_profile.svg/250px-Milky_way_profile.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Milky_way_profile.svg/375px-Milky_way_profile.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Milky_way_profile.svg/500px-Milky_way_profile.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Milky_way_profile.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A schematic profile of the Milky Way</div></div></div>\n<p>Estimates of the mass of the Milky Way vary, depending upon the method and data used. The low end of the estimate range is 5.8<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">11</span></sup>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass">solar masses</a> (<var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub>), somewhat less than that of the <a href="/wiki/Andromeda_Galaxy" title="Andromeda Galaxy">Andromeda Galaxy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karachentsevetal2006_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Karachentsevetal2006-45">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mass_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-mass-46">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-galmass_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-galmass-47">[45]</a></sup> Measurements using the <a href="/wiki/Very_Long_Baseline_Array" title="Very Long Baseline Array">Very Long Baseline Array</a> in 2009 found velocities as large as 254&#160;km/s (570,000&#160;mph) for stars at the outer edge of the Milky Way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nrao20090105_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-nrao20090105-48">[46]</a></sup> Because the orbital velocity depends on the total mass inside the orbital radius, this suggests that the Milky Way is more massive, roughly equaling the mass of Andromeda Galaxy at 7<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">11</span></sup>&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub> within 160,000&#160;ly (49&#160;kpc) of its center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reid2009_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-reid2009-49">[47]</a></sup> In 2010, a measurement of the radial velocity of halo stars found that the mass enclosed within 80 kilo<a href="/wiki/Parsec" title="Parsec">parsecs</a> is 7<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">11</span></sup>&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gnedin2010_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-gnedin2010-50">[48]</a></sup> According to a study published in 2014, the mass of the entire Milky Way is estimated to be 8.5<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">11</span></sup>&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jorge_Pe&#241;arrubia2014_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jorge_Pe&#241;arrubia2014-51">[49]</a></sup> but this is actually only half the mass of the Andromeda Galaxy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jorge_Pe&#241;arrubia2014_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jorge_Pe&#241;arrubia2014-51">[49]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Much of the mass of the Milky Way appears to be <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a>, an unknown and invisible form of matter that interacts gravitationally with ordinary matter. A <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter_halo" title="Dark matter halo">dark matter halo</a> is conjectured to spread out relatively uniformly to a distance beyond one hundred kiloparsecs (kpc) from the Galactic Center. Mathematical models of the Milky Way suggest that the mass of dark matter is 1&#8211;1.5<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">12</span></sup>&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McMillan2011_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-McMillan2011-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McMillan2016_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-McMillan2016-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[50]</a></sup> Recent studies indicate a range in mass, as large as 4.5<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">12</span></sup>&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup> and as small as 8<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">11</span></sup>&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[52]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The total mass of all the stars in the Milky Way is estimated to be between 4.6<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">10</span></sup>&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Licquia2013_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Licquia2013-55">[53]</a></sup> and 6.43<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">10</span></sup>&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McMillan2011_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-McMillan2011-6">[6]</a></sup> In addition to the stars, there is also interstellar gas, comprising 90% <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and 10% <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> by mass,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ism1_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-ism1-56">[54]</a></sup> with two thirds of the hydrogen found in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutral_hydrogen" title="Neutral hydrogen">atomic form</a> and the remaining one-third as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_hydrogen" title="Molecular hydrogen">molecular hydrogen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ism2_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-ism2-57">[55]</a></sup> The mass of this gas is equal to between 10%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ism2_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-ism2-57">[55]</a></sup> and 15%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ism1_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-ism1-56">[54]</a></sup> of the total mass of the galaxy\'s stars. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstellar_dust" title="Interstellar dust">Interstellar dust</a> accounts for an additional 1% of the total mass of the gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ism1_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-ism1-56">[54]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Contents">Contents</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Exoplanet" title="Exoplanet">Exoplanet</a></div>\n<p>The Milky Way contains between 200 and 400&#160;billion stars<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frommert_kronberg2005_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-frommert_kronberg2005-58">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wethington2010_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-wethington2010-59">[57]</a></sup> and at least 100&#160;billion planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-villard20120111_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-villard20120111-60">[58]</a></sup> The exact figure depends on the number of very-low-mass stars, which are hard to detect, especially at distances of more than 300&#160;ly (90&#160;pc) from the Sun. As a comparison, the neighboring Andromeda Galaxy contains an estimated one trillion (10<sup>12</sup>) stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-young2006_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-young2006-61">[59]</a></sup> The Milky Way may also contain perhaps ten billion <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarfs</a>, a billion <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">neutron stars</a>, and a hundred million <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black holes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[nb 3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[62]</a></sup> Filling the space between the stars is a disk of gas and dust called the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a>. This disk has at least a comparable extent in radius to the stars,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science312_1773_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-science312_1773-66">[63]</a></sup> whereas the thickness of the gas layer ranges from hundreds of light years for the colder gas to thousands of light years for warmer gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-araa28_215_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-araa28_215-67">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apj702_1472_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-apj702_1472-68">[65]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The disk of stars in the Milky Way does not have a sharp edge beyond which there are no stars. Rather, the concentration of stars decreases with distance from the center of the Milky Way. For reasons that are not understood, beyond a radius of roughly 40,000 ly (13 kpc) from the center, the number of stars per cubic <a href="/wiki/Parsec" title="Parsec">parsec</a> drops much faster with radius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arxiv0909.3857_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-arxiv0909.3857-69">[66]</a></sup> Surrounding the galactic disk is a spherical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galactic_Halo" title="Galactic Halo">Galactic Halo</a> of stars and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Globular_clusters" title="Globular clusters">globular clusters</a> that extends further outward but is limited in size by the orbits of two Milky Way satellites, the Large and Small <a href="/wiki/Magellanic_Clouds" title="Magellanic Clouds">Magellanic Clouds</a>, whose <a href="/wiki/Apsis" title="Apsis">closest approach</a> to the Galactic Center is about 180,000&#160;ly (55&#160;kpc).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mnras371_1_108_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-mnras371_1_108-70">[67]</a></sup> At this distance or beyond, the orbits of most halo objects would be disrupted by the Magellanic Clouds. Hence, such objects would probably be ejected from the vicinity of the Milky Way. The integrated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absolute_visual_magnitude" title="Absolute visual magnitude">absolute visual magnitude</a> of the Milky Way is estimated to be around &#8722;20.9.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coffey_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-coffey-71">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karachentsev2004_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Karachentsev2004-72">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-milky_way_mag_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-milky_way_mag-73">[a]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Both <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_microlensing" title="Gravitational microlensing">gravitational microlensing</a> and planetary transit observations indicate that there may be at least as many planets bound to stars as there are stars in the Milky Way,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature-20120111_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nature-20120111-29">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-borenstein2011_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-borenstein2011-74">[70]</a></sup> and microlensing measurements indicate that there are more <a href="/wiki/Rogue_planet" title="Rogue planet">rogue planets</a> not bound to host stars than there are stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa20110526_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa20110526-76">[72]</a></sup> The Milky Way contains at least one planet per star, resulting in 100&#8211;400 billion planets, according to a January 2013 study of the five-planet star system <a href="/wiki/Kepler-32" title="Kepler-32">Kepler-32</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Kepler_(spacecraft)" title="Kepler (spacecraft)">Kepler</a> space observatory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space-20130102_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Space-20130102-30">[29]</a></sup> A different January 2013 analysis of Kepler data estimated that at least 17 billion <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">Earth-sized</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exoplanets" title="Exoplanets">exoplanets</a> reside in the Milky Way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space-Billions_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Space-Billions-77">[73]</a></sup> On November 4, 2013, astronomers reported, based on <a href="/wiki/Kepler_(spacecraft)" title="Kepler (spacecraft)"><i>Kepler</i> space mission</a> data, that there could be as many as 40 billion Earth-sized <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extrasolar_planet" title="Extrasolar planet">planets</a> orbiting in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitable_zone" title="Habitable zone">habitable zones</a> of <a href="/wiki/Solar_analog" title="Solar analog">Sun-like stars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Red_dwarf" title="Red dwarf">red dwarfs</a> within the Milky Way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20131104_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20131104-78">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PNAS-20131031_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-PNAS-20131031-79">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nov2013_planet_estimate_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-nov2013_planet_estimate-80">[76]</a></sup> 11 billion of these estimated planets may be orbiting Sun-like stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LATimes-20131104_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-LATimes-20131104-81">[77]</a></sup> The nearest such planet may be 4.2 light-years away, according to a 2016 study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planet-Alpha-Centauri_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Planet-Alpha-Centauri-82">[78]</a></sup> Such Earth-sized planets may be more numerous than gas giants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature-20120111_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nature-20120111-29">[28]</a></sup> Besides exoplanets, "<a href="/wiki/Exocomet" title="Exocomet">exocomets</a>", <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comets" title="Comets">comets</a> beyond the Solar System, have also been detected and may be common in the Milky Way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space-Exocomets_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Space-Exocomets-83">[79]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed-hover center">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 552px"><div style="width: 552px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 550px;"><div style="margin:-0.16666666666666px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ESO_-_Milky_Way.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="6000" decoding="async" height="276" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/ESO_-_Milky_Way.jpg/825px-ESO_-_Milky_Way.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/ESO_-_Milky_Way.jpg/1238px-ESO_-_Milky_Way.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/ESO_-_Milky_Way.jpg/1650px-ESO_-_Milky_Way.jpg 2x" width="550"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytextwrapper" style="width: 530px"><div class="gallerytext">\n<p>360-degree panorama view of the Milky Way (an assembled mosaic of photographs) by <a href="/wiki/European_Southern_Observatory" title="European Southern Observatory">ESO</a>. The galactic centre is in the middle of the view, with galactic north up.\n</p>\n</div></div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_2" style="width:220px;" videopayload=\'&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:854px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_3" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Artist%27s_impression_of_the_Milky_Way.ogv/854px--Artist%27s_impression_of_the_Milky_Way.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:854px;height:480px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="26.120158730159" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Artist&amp;#039;s_impression_of_the_Milky_Way.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/5/55/Artist%27s_impression_of_the_Milky_Way.ogv/Artist%27s_impression_of_the_Milky_Way.ogv.480p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; From edge-on lines-of-sight, the (peanut shell)-shaped structure, not to be confused with the galaxy\'s central bulge, is evident. Viewed from above, the central narrow bar that is responsible for this structure appears clearly, as would many spiral arms and their associated dust clouds.</div></div></div>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Artist%27s_impression_of_the_Milky_Way_(updated_-_annotated).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="5600" data-file-width="5600" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Artist%27s_impression_of_the_Milky_Way_%28updated_-_annotated%29.jpg/220px-Artist%27s_impression_of_the_Milky_Way_%28updated_-_annotated%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Artist%27s_impression_of_the_Milky_Way_%28updated_-_annotated%29.jpg/330px-Artist%27s_impression_of_the_Milky_Way_%28updated_-_annotated%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Artist%27s_impression_of_the_Milky_Way_%28updated_-_annotated%29.jpg/440px-Artist%27s_impression_of_the_Milky_Way_%28updated_-_annotated%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Artist\'s conception of the spiral structure of the Milky Way with two major stellar arms and a bar<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-two_arms_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-two_arms-84">[80]</a></sup></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Milky_Way_IR_Spitzer.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2525" data-file-width="3501" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Milky_Way_IR_Spitzer.jpg/220px-Milky_Way_IR_Spitzer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Milky_Way_IR_Spitzer.jpg/330px-Milky_Way_IR_Spitzer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Milky_Way_IR_Spitzer.jpg/440px-Milky_Way_IR_Spitzer.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><i><a href="/wiki/Spitzer_Space_Telescope" title="Spitzer Space Telescope">Spitzer</a></i> reveals what cannot be seen in visible light: cooler stars (blue), heated dust (reddish hue), and <a href="/wiki/Sagittarius_A*" title="Sagittarius A*">Sgr A*</a> as bright white spot in the middle.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:X-RayFlare-BlackHole-MilkyWay-20140105.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/X-RayFlare-BlackHole-MilkyWay-20140105.jpg/220px-X-RayFlare-BlackHole-MilkyWay-20140105.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/X-RayFlare-BlackHole-MilkyWay-20140105.jpg/330px-X-RayFlare-BlackHole-MilkyWay-20140105.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/X-RayFlare-BlackHole-MilkyWay-20140105.jpg/440px-X-RayFlare-BlackHole-MilkyWay-20140105.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Bright <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a> flares from <a href="/wiki/Sagittarius_A*" title="Sagittarius A*">Sagittarius A*</a>, location of the <a href="/wiki/Supermassive_black_hole" title="Supermassive black hole">supermassive black hole</a> at the center of the Milky Way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150105_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150105-85">[81]</a></sup></div></div></div></div>\n<p>The Milky Way consists of a bar-shaped core region surrounded by a disk of <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">gas, dust</a> and stars. The mass distribution within the Milky Way closely resembles the type Sbc in the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_sequence" title="Hubble sequence">Hubble classification</a>, which represents spiral galaxies with relatively loosely wound arms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ssr100_1_129_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-ssr100_1_129-1">[1]</a></sup> Astronomers first began to suspect that the Milky Way is a <a href="/wiki/Barred_spiral_galaxy" title="Barred spiral galaxy">barred spiral galaxy</a>, rather than an ordinary <a href="/wiki/Spiral_galaxy" title="Spiral galaxy">spiral galaxy</a>, in the 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[84]</a></sup> Their suspicions were confirmed by the <a href="/wiki/Spitzer_Space_Telescope" title="Spitzer Space Telescope">Spitzer Space Telescope</a> observations in 2005<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fn3_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-fn3-89">[85]</a></sup> that showed the Milky Way\'s central bar to be larger than previously thought.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Galactic_quadrants">Galactic quadrants</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Galactic_quadrant" title="Galactic quadrant">Galactic quadrant</a></div>\n<p>A galactic quadrant, or quadrant of the Milky Way, refers to one of four circular sectors in the division of the Milky Way. In actual astronomical practice, the delineation of the galactic quadrants is based upon the <a href="/wiki/Galactic_coordinate_system" title="Galactic coordinate system">galactic coordinate system</a>, which places the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_coordinates" title="Polar coordinates">origin of the mapping system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[86]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Quadrants are described using <a href="/wiki/Ordinal_number_(linguistics)" title="Ordinal number (linguistics)">ordinals</a>&#8212;for example, "1st galactic quadrant",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toolsofradio_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-toolsofradio-91">[87]</a></sup> "second galactic quadrant",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASAAD131K_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASAAD131K-92">[88]</a></sup> or "third quadrant of the Milky Way".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lampton1997_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lampton1997-93">[89]</a></sup> Viewing from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_galactic_pole" title="North galactic pole">north galactic pole</a> with 0 <a href="/wiki/Degree_(angle)" title="Degree (angle)">degrees</a> (&#176;) as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ray_(geometry)" title="Ray (geometry)">ray</a> that runs starting from the Sun and through the Galactic Center, the quadrants are as follows:\n</p>\n<ul><li>1st galactic quadrant&#160;&#8211; 0&#176; &#8804; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galactic_longitude" title="Galactic longitude">longitude</a> (&#8467;) &#8804; 90&#176;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[90]</a></sup></li>\n<li>2nd galactic quadrant&#160;&#8211; 90&#176; &#8804; &#8467; &#8804; 180&#176;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASAAD131K_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASAAD131K-92">[88]</a></sup></li>\n<li>3rd galactic quadrant&#160;&#8211; 180&#176; &#8804; &#8467; &#8804; 270&#176;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lampton1997_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lampton1997-93">[89]</a></sup></li>\n<li>4th galactic quadrant&#160;&#8211; 270&#176; &#8804; &#8467; &#8804; 360&#176; (0&#176;)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toolsofradio_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-toolsofradio-91">[87]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Galactic_Center">Galactic Center</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Galactic_Center" title="Galactic Center">Galactic Center</a></div>\n<p>The Sun is 25,000&#8211;28,000&#160;ly (7.7&#8211;8.6&#160;kpc) from the Galactic Center. This value is estimated using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geometric" title="Geometric">geometric</a>-based methods or by measuring selected astronomical objects that serve as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_candles" title="Standard candles">standard candles</a>, with different techniques yielding various values within this approximate range.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apj692_2_1075_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-apj692_2_1075-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boehle2016_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-boehle2016-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gillessen2016_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gillessen2016-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReideaSgrb2009_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReideaSgrb2009-95">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vanhollebeke09_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-vanhollebeke09-96">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-majaess10_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-majaess10-97">[93]</a></sup> In the inner few kpc (around 10,000 light-years radius) is a dense concentration of mostly old stars in a roughly spheroidal shape called <a href="/wiki/Bulge_(astronomy)" title="Bulge (astronomy)">the bulge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Galactic_bulge_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Galactic_bulge-98">[94]</a></sup> It has been proposed that the Milky Way lacks a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galactic_bulge#Classical_bulges" title="Galactic bulge">bulge</a> formed due to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galactic_merger" title="Galactic merger">collision and merger between previous galaxies</a>, and that instead it only has a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galactic_bulge#Disk-like_Bulges" title="Galactic bulge"><i>pseudobulge</i></a> formed by its central bar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apjl_1_l72_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-apjl_1_l72-99">[95]</a></sup> However, confusion in the literature between the (peanut shell)-shaped structure created by instabilities in the bar, versus a possible <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galactic_bulge#Classical_bulges" title="Galactic bulge">bulge</a> with an expected half-light radius of 0.5 kpc,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[96]</a></sup> abound.\n</p><p>The Galactic Center is marked by an intense <a href="/wiki/Radio_astronomy" title="Radio astronomy">radio source</a> named <a href="/wiki/Sagittarius_A*" title="Sagittarius A*">Sagittarius A*</a> (pronounced Sagittarius A-star). The motion of material around the center indicates that Sagittarius A* harbors a massive, compact object.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones2004_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2004-101">[97]</a></sup> This concentration of mass is best explained as a <a href="/wiki/Supermassive_black_hole" title="Supermassive black hole">supermassive black hole</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chandra2003_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-chandra2003-102">[nb 4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apj692_2_1075_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-apj692_2_1075-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apj689_2_1044_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-apj689_2_1044-103">[98]</a></sup> (SMBH) with an estimated mass of 4.1&#8211;4.5 million times the <a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass">mass of the Sun</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apj689_2_1044_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-apj689_2_1044-103">[98]</a></sup> The rate of accretion of the SMBH is consistent with an <a href="/wiki/Active_galactic_nucleus" title="Active galactic nucleus">inactive galactic nucleus</a>, being estimated at around <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6995100000000000000&#9824;</span>1<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>&#8722;5</sup></span>&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub>&#160;y<sup>&#8722;1</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wang13_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-wang13-104">[99]</a></sup> (around 100 solar masses per year). Observations indicate that there are SMBHs located near the center of most normal galaxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blandford1999_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-blandford1999-105">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frolov_zelnikov2011_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-frolov_zelnikov2011-106">[101]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The nature of the Milky Way\'s bar is actively debated, with estimates for its half-length and orientation spanning from 1&#160;to 5&#160;kpc (3,000&#8211;16,000&#160;ly) and 10&#8211;50 degrees relative to the line of sight from Earth to the Galactic Center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vanhollebeke09_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-vanhollebeke09-96">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-majaess10_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-majaess10-97">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cabrera-Lavers08_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cabrera-Lavers08-107">[102]</a></sup> Certain authors advocate that the Milky Way features two distinct bars, one nestled within the other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nishiyama06_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-nishiyama06-108">[103]</a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/RR_Lyrae_variable" title="RR Lyrae variable">RR Lyrae variables</a> do not trace a prominent Galactic bar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-majaess10_97-2"><a href="#cite_note-majaess10-97">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alcock98_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-alcock98-109">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kunder08_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-kunder08-110">[105]</a></sup> The bar may be surrounded by a ring called the "5-kpc ring" that contains a large fraction of the molecular hydrogen present in the Milky Way, as well as most of the Milky Way\'s <a href="/wiki/Star_formation" title="Star formation">star formation</a> activity. Viewed from the Andromeda Galaxy, it would be the brightest feature of the Milky Way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fn14_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-fn14-111">[106]</a></sup> X-ray emission from the core is aligned with the massive stars surrounding the central bar<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wang13_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-wang13-104">[99]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Galactic_ridge" title="Galactic ridge">Galactic ridge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature318_267_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature318_267-112">[107]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:800_nasa_structure_renderin2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1629" data-file-width="2896" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/800_nasa_structure_renderin2.jpg/220px-800_nasa_structure_renderin2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/800_nasa_structure_renderin2.jpg/330px-800_nasa_structure_renderin2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/800_nasa_structure_renderin2.jpg/440px-800_nasa_structure_renderin2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:800_nasa_structure_renderin2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration of the two gigantic <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamma-ray" title="Gamma-ray">gamma-ray</a> bubbles (blue-violet) of the Milky Way (center)</div></div></div>\n<p>In 2010, two gigantic spherical bubbles of high energy emission were detected to the north and the south of the Milky Way core, using data from the <a href="/wiki/Fermi_Gamma-ray_Space_Telescope" title="Fermi Gamma-ray Space Telescope">Fermi Gamma-ray Space Telescope</a>. The diameter of each of the bubbles is about 25,000 light-years (7.7&#160;kpc); they stretch up to <a href="/wiki/Grus_(constellation)" title="Grus (constellation)">Grus</a> and to <a href="/wiki/Virgo_(constellation)" title="Virgo (constellation)">Virgo</a> on the night-sky of the southern hemisphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-overbye2010_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-overbye2010-113">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sz2010_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-sz2010-114">[109]</a></sup> Subsequently, observations with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parkes_Telescope" title="Parkes Telescope">Parkes Telescope</a> at radio frequencies identified polarized emission that is associated with the Fermi bubbles. These observations are best interpreted as a magnetized outflow driven by star formation in the central 640&#160;ly (200&#160;pc) of the Milky Way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[110]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Later, on January 5, 2015, <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> reported observing an <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a> flare 400 times brighter than usual, a record-breaker, from Sagittarius A*. The unusual event may have been caused by the breaking apart of an <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> falling into the black hole or by the entanglement of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_field_lines" title="Magnetic field lines">magnetic field lines</a> within gas flowing into Sagittarius A*.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150105_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150105-85">[81]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spiral_arms">Spiral arms</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Spiral_galaxy" title="Spiral galaxy">Spiral galaxy</a></div>\n<p>Outside the gravitational influence of the Galactic bars, the structure of the interstellar medium and stars in the disk of the Milky Way is organized into four spiral arms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-churchwell09_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-churchwell09-116">[111]</a></sup> Spiral arms typically contain a higher density of interstellar gas and dust than the Galactic average as well as a greater concentration of star formation, as traced by <a href="/wiki/H_II_region" title="H II region">H II regions</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TaylorCordes_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-TaylorCordes-117">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-russeil03_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-russeil03-118">[113]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloud" title="Molecular cloud">molecular clouds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[114]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Milky Way\'s spiral structure is uncertain, and there is currently no consensus on the nature of the Milky Way\'s spiral arms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-two_arms_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-two_arms-84">[80]</a></sup> Perfect logarithmic spiral patterns only crudely describe features near the Sun,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-russeil03_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-russeil03-118">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-majaess09b_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-majaess09b-120">[115]</a></sup> because galaxies commonly have arms that branch, merge, twist unexpectedly, and feature a degree of irregularity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-majaess10_97-3"><a href="#cite_note-majaess10-97">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-majaess09b_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-majaess09b-120">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lepine10_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-lepine10-121">[116]</a></sup> The possible scenario of the Sun within a spur / Local arm<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-russeil03_118-2"><a href="#cite_note-russeil03-118">[113]</a></sup> emphasizes that point and indicates that such features are probably not unique, and exist elsewhere in the Milky Way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-majaess09b_120-2"><a href="#cite_note-majaess09b-120">[115]</a></sup> Estimates of the pitch angle of the arms range from about 7&#176; to 25&#176;.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science312_1773_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-science312_1773-66">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drimmel2000_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Drimmel2000-122">[117]</a></sup> There are thought to be four spiral arms that all start near the Milky Way\'s center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[118]</a></sup> These are named as follows, with the positions of the arms shown in the image at right:\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Milky_Way_Arms.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="580" data-file-width="614" decoding="async" height="472" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Milky_Way_Arms.svg/500px-Milky_Way_Arms.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Milky_Way_Arms.svg/750px-Milky_Way_Arms.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Milky_Way_Arms.svg/1000px-Milky_Way_Arms.svg.png 2x" width="500"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Milky_Way_Arms.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Observed (normal lines) and extrapolated (dotted lines) structure of the spiral arms of the Milky Way, viewed from "north" of the galaxy. Stars generally move clockwise in this view. The gray lines radiating from the Sun\'s position (upper center) list the three-letter abbreviations of the corresponding constellations.</div></div></div>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Color\n</th>\n<th>Arm(s)\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="background:#28c7b4;">cyan\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Near_3_kpc_Arm" title="Near 3 kpc Arm">Near 3 kpc Arm</a> and <a href="/wiki/Perseus_Arm" title="Perseus Arm">Perseus Arm</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="background:#928bff;">purple\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Norma_Arm" title="Norma Arm">Norma</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Outer_arm" title="Outer arm">Outer arm</a> (Along with extension discovered in 2004<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcclure-griffiths_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-mcclure-griffiths-124">[119]</a></sup>)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="background:#07c459;">green\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Scutum%E2%80%93Centaurus_Arm" title="Scutum&#8211;Centaurus Arm">Scutum&#8211;Centaurus Arm</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="background:#ff6c6c;">pink\n</td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sagittarius-Carina_Arm" title="Sagittarius-Carina Arm">Sagittarius-Carina Arm</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;"><i>There are at least two smaller arms or spurs, including:</i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="background:#ff9b00;">orange\n</td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orion%E2%80%93Cygnus_Arm" title="Orion&#8211;Cygnus Arm">Orion&#8211;Cygnus Arm</a> (which contains the Sun and Solar System)\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Two spiral arms, the Scutum&#8211;Centaurus arm and the Carina&#8211;Sagittarius arm, have tangent points inside the Sun\'s orbit about the center of the Milky Way. If these arms contain an overdensity of stars compared to the average density of stars in the Galactic disk, it would be detectable by counting the stars near the tangent point. Two surveys of near-infrared light, which is sensitive primarily to red giants and not affected by dust extinction, detected the predicted overabundance in the Scutum&#8211;Centaurus arm but not in the Carina&#8211;Sagittarius arm: the Scutum-Centaurus Arm contains approximately 30% more <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">red giants</a> than would be expected in the absence of a spiral arm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drimmel2000_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-Drimmel2000-122">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benjamin2005_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Benjamin2005-125">[120]</a></sup> This observation suggests that the Milky Way possesses only two major stellar arms: the Perseus arm and the Scutum&#8211;Centaurus arm. The rest of the arms contain excess gas but not excess old stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-two_arms_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-two_arms-84">[80]</a></sup> In December 2013, astronomers found that the distribution of young stars and star-forming regions matches the four-arm spiral description of the Milky Way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urquart2013_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-urquart2013-128">[123]</a></sup> Thus, the Milky Way appears to have two spiral arms as traced by old stars and four spiral arms as traced by gas and young stars. The explanation for this apparent discrepancy is unclear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urquart2013_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-urquart2013-128">[123]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19341-MilkyWayGalaxy-SpiralArmsData-WISE-20150603.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1308" data-file-width="1308" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/PIA19341-MilkyWayGalaxy-SpiralArmsData-WISE-20150603.jpg/200px-PIA19341-MilkyWayGalaxy-SpiralArmsData-WISE-20150603.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/PIA19341-MilkyWayGalaxy-SpiralArmsData-WISE-20150603.jpg/300px-PIA19341-MilkyWayGalaxy-SpiralArmsData-WISE-20150603.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/PIA19341-MilkyWayGalaxy-SpiralArmsData-WISE-20150603.jpg/400px-PIA19341-MilkyWayGalaxy-SpiralArmsData-WISE-20150603.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA19341-MilkyWayGalaxy-SpiralArmsData-WISE-20150603.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Clusters detected by <a href="/wiki/Wide-field_Infrared_Survey_Explorer" title="Wide-field Infrared Survey Explorer">WISE</a> used to trace the Milky Way\'s spiral arms</div></div></div>\n<p>The Near 3 kpc Arm (also called Expanding 3 kpc Arm or simply 3 kpc Arm) was discovered in the 1950s by astronomer van Woerden and collaborators through <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_line" title="Hydrogen line">21-centimeter</a> radio measurements of HI (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_hydrogen" title="Atomic hydrogen">atomic hydrogen</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dame_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dame-130">[125]</a></sup> It was found to be expanding away from the central bulge at more than 50 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilometer_per_second" title="Kilometer per second">km/s</a>. It is located in the fourth galactic quadrant at a distance of about 5.2 <a href="/wiki/Parsec" title="Parsec">kpc</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> and 3.3 kpc from the <a href="/wiki/Galactic_Center" title="Galactic Center">Galactic Center</a>. The Far 3 kpc Arm was discovered in 2008 by astronomer Tom Dame (Harvard-Smithsonian CfA). It is located in the first galactic quadrant at a distance of 3 <a href="/wiki/Parsec" title="Parsec">kpc</a> (about 10,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_year" title="Light year">ly</a>) from the Galactic Center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dame_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dame-130">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[126]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A simulation published in 2011 suggested that the Milky Way may have obtained its spiral arm structure as a result of repeated collisions with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sagittarius_Dwarf_Elliptical_Galaxy" title="Sagittarius Dwarf Elliptical Galaxy">Sagittarius Dwarf Elliptical Galaxy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[127]</a></sup>\n</p><p>It has been suggested that the Milky Way contains two different spiral patterns: an inner one, formed by the Sagittarius arm, that rotates fast and an outer one, formed by the Carina and Perseus arms, whose rotation velocity is slower and whose arms are tightly wound. In this scenario, suggested by numerical simulations of the dynamics of the different spiral arms, the outer pattern would form an outer <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_morphological_classification#De_Vaucouleurs_system" title="Galaxy morphological classification">pseudoring</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pseudoring2_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-pseudoring2-133">[128]</a></sup> and the two patterns would be connected by the Cygnus arm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pseudoring_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-pseudoring-134">[129]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nessie_molecular_cloud.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="723" data-file-width="2501" decoding="async" height="64" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Nessie_molecular_cloud.png/220px-Nessie_molecular_cloud.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Nessie_molecular_cloud.png/330px-Nessie_molecular_cloud.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Nessie_molecular_cloud.png/440px-Nessie_molecular_cloud.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nessie_molecular_cloud.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The long filamentary molecular cloud dubbed "Nessie" probably forms a dense "spine" of the Scutum&#8211;Centarus Arm</div></div></div>\n<p>Outside of the major spiral arms is the <a href="/wiki/Monoceros_Ring" title="Monoceros Ring">Monoceros Ring</a> (or Outer Ring), a ring of gas and stars torn from other galaxies billions of years ago. However, several members of the scientific community recently restated their position affirming the Monoceros structure is nothing more than an over-density produced by the flared and warped <a href="/wiki/Thick_disk" title="Thick disk">thick disk</a> of the Milky Way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[130]</a></sup> The structure of the Milky Way\'s disk is warped along an <a href="/wiki/Sigmoid_function" title="Sigmoid function">"S" curve</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[131]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Halo">Halo</span></h3>\n<p>The Galactic disk is surrounded by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galactic_spheroid" title="Galactic spheroid">spheroidal halo</a> of old stars and globular clusters, of which 90% lie within 100,000 light-years (30&#160;kpc) of the Galactic Center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harris2003_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-harris2003-137">[132]</a></sup> However, a few globular clusters have been found farther, such as PAL 4 and AM1 at more than 200,000 light-years from the Galactic Center. About 40% of the Milky Way\'s clusters are on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrograde_orbit" title="Retrograde orbit">retrograde orbits</a>, which means they move in the opposite direction from the Milky Way rotation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aaa313_119_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-aaa313_119-138">[133]</a></sup> The globular clusters can follow <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rosette_orbit" title="Rosette orbit">rosette orbits</a> about the Milky Way, in contrast to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elliptical_orbit" title="Elliptical orbit">elliptical orbit</a> of a planet around a star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apj522_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-apj522-139">[134]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although the disk contains dust that obscures the view in some wavelengths, the halo component does not. Active <a href="/wiki/Star_formation" title="Star formation">star formation</a> takes place in the disk (especially in the spiral arms, which represent areas of high density), but does not take place in the halo, as there is little gas cool enough to collapse into stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sparke_gallagher_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-sparke_gallagher-14">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Open_cluster" title="Open cluster">Open clusters</a> are also located primarily in the disk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aj108_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-aj108-140">[135]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Discoveries in the early 21st century have added dimension to the knowledge of the Milky Way\'s structure. With the discovery that the disk of the Andromeda Galaxy (M31) extends much further than previously thought,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fn5_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-fn5-141">[136]</a></sup> the possibility of the disk of the Milky Way extending further is apparent, and this is supported by evidence from the discovery of the Outer Arm extension of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cygnus_Arm" title="Cygnus Arm">Cygnus Arm</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcclure-griffiths_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-mcclure-griffiths-124">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fn6_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-fn6-142">[137]</a></sup> and of a similar extension of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scutum-Centaurus_Arm" title="Scutum-Centaurus Arm">Scutum-Centaurus Arm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[138]</a></sup> With the discovery of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sagittarius_Dwarf_Elliptical_Galaxy" title="Sagittarius Dwarf Elliptical Galaxy">Sagittarius Dwarf Elliptical Galaxy</a> came the discovery of a ribbon of galactic debris as the polar orbit of the dwarf and its interaction with the Milky Way tears it apart. Similarly, with the discovery of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canis_Major_Dwarf_Galaxy" title="Canis Major Dwarf Galaxy">Canis Major Dwarf Galaxy</a>, it was found that a ring of galactic debris from its interaction with the Milky Way encircles the Galactic disk.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Sloan_Digital_Sky_Survey" title="Sloan Digital Sky Survey">Sloan Digital Sky Survey</a> of the northern sky shows a huge and diffuse structure (spread out across an area around 5,000 times the size of a full moon) within the Milky Way that does not seem to fit within current models. The collection of stars rises close to perpendicular to the plane of the spiral arms of the Milky Way. The proposed likely interpretation is that a <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_galaxy" title="Dwarf galaxy">dwarf galaxy</a> is merging with the Milky Way. This galaxy is tentatively named the <a href="/wiki/Virgo_Stellar_Stream" title="Virgo Stellar Stream">Virgo Stellar Stream</a> and is found in the direction of Virgo about 30,000 light-years (9&#160;kpc) away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apj673_2_864_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-apj673_2_864-144">[139]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gaseous_halo">Gaseous halo</span></h4>\n<p>In addition to the stellar halo, the <a href="/wiki/Chandra_X-ray_Observatory" title="Chandra X-ray Observatory">Chandra X-ray Observatory</a>, <a href="/wiki/XMM-Newton" title="XMM-Newton">XMM-Newton</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Suzaku_(satellite)" title="Suzaku (satellite)"><i>Suzaku</i></a> have provided evidence that there is a gaseous halo with a large amount of hot gas. The halo extends for hundreds of thousand of light years, much further than the stellar halo and close to the distance of the Large and Small <a href="/wiki/Magellanic_Clouds" title="Magellanic Clouds">Magellanic Clouds</a>. The mass of this hot halo is nearly equivalent to the mass of the Milky Way itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chandra_hotgas_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-chandra_hotgas-145">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[142]</a></sup> The temperature of this halo gas is between 1 and 2.5&#160;million&#160;K (1.8 and 4.5&#160;million&#160;<sup>o</sup>F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[143]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Observations of distant galaxies indicate that the Universe had about one-sixth as much <a href="/wiki/Baryon" title="Baryon">baryonic</a> (ordinary) matter as dark matter when it was just a few billion years old. However, only about half of those baryons are accounted for in the modern Universe based on observations of nearby galaxies like the Milky Way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harrington_feature_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harrington_feature-149">[144]</a></sup> If the finding that the mass of the halo is comparable to the mass of the Milky Way is confirmed, it could be the identity of the missing baryons around the Milky Way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harrington_feature_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-Harrington_feature-149">[144]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Sun.E2.80.99s_location_and_neighborhood"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Sun&#8217;s_location_and_neighborhood">Sun&#8217;s location and neighborhood</span></h3>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galactic_longitude.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="1134" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Galactic_longitude.JPG/220px-Galactic_longitude.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Galactic_longitude.JPG/330px-Galactic_longitude.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Galactic_longitude.JPG/440px-Galactic_longitude.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Diagram of the Sun&#8217;s location in the Milky Way. The angles represent longitudes in the <a href="/wiki/Galactic_coordinate_system" title="Galactic coordinate system">galactic coordinate system</a>.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:04-Closest_Stars_(LofE04240).png"><img alt="" data-file-height="3786" data-file-width="5172" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/04-Closest_Stars_%28LofE04240%29.png/220px-04-Closest_Stars_%28LofE04240%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/04-Closest_Stars_%28LofE04240%29.png/330px-04-Closest_Stars_%28LofE04240%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/04-Closest_Stars_%28LofE04240%29.png/440px-04-Closest_Stars_%28LofE04240%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Diagram of the stars in the Solar neighborhood</div></div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> is near the inner rim of the <a href="/wiki/Orion_Arm" title="Orion Arm">Orion Arm</a>, within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Local_Fluff" title="Local Fluff">Local Fluff</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Local_Bubble" title="Local Bubble">Local Bubble</a>, and in the <a href="/wiki/Gould_Belt" title="Gould Belt">Gould Belt</a>, at a distance of 26.4&#160;&#177;&#160;1.0&#160;kly (8.09&#160;&#177;&#160;0.31&#160;kpc)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apj692_2_1075_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-apj692_2_1075-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boehle2016_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-boehle2016-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gillessen2016_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gillessen2016-13">[13]</a></sup> from the Galactic Center. The Sun is currently 5&#8211;30 parsecs (16&#8211;98&#160;ly) from the central plane of the Galactic disk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-majaess2009_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-majaess2009-150">[145]</a></sup> The distance between the local arm and the next arm out, the <a href="/wiki/Perseus_Arm" title="Perseus Arm">Perseus Arm</a>, is about 2,000 parsecs (6,500&#160;ly).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fn9_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-fn9-151">[146]</a></sup> The Sun, and thus the Solar System, is located in the Milky Way\'s <a href="/wiki/Galactic_habitable_zone" title="Galactic habitable zone">galactic habitable zone</a>.\n</p><p>There are about 208 stars brighter than <a href="/wiki/Absolute_magnitude" title="Absolute magnitude">absolute magnitude</a> 8.5 within a sphere with a radius of 15 parsecs (49&#160;ly) from the Sun, giving a density of one star per 69 cubic parsecs, or one star per 2,360 cubic light-years (from <a href="/wiki/List_of_nearest_bright_stars" title="List of nearest bright stars">List of nearest bright stars</a>). On the other hand, there are 64 known stars (of any magnitude, not counting 4 <a href="/wiki/Brown_dwarf" title="Brown dwarf">brown dwarfs</a>) within 5 parsecs (16&#160;ly) of the Sun, giving a density of about one star per 8.2 cubic parsecs, or one per 284 cubic light-years (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_nearest_stars" title="List of nearest stars">List of nearest stars</a>). This illustrates the fact that there are far more faint stars than bright stars: in the entire sky, there are about 500 stars brighter than <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitude</a> 4 but 15.5 million stars brighter than apparent magnitude 14.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[147]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The apex of the Sun\'s way, or the <a href="/wiki/Solar_apex" title="Solar apex">solar apex</a>, is the direction that the Sun travels through space in the Milky Way. The general direction of the Sun\'s Galactic motion is towards the star <a href="/wiki/Vega" title="Vega">Vega</a> near the constellation of <a href="/wiki/Hercules_(constellation)" title="Hercules (constellation)">Hercules</a>, at an angle of roughly 60 sky degrees to the direction of the Galactic Center. The Sun\'s orbit about the Milky Way is expected to be roughly elliptical with the addition of perturbations due to the Galactic spiral arms and non-uniform mass distributions. In addition, the Sun passes through the Galactic plane approximately 2.7 times per orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[148]</a></sup> This is very similar to how a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simple_harmonic_oscillator" title="Simple harmonic oscillator">simple harmonic oscillator</a> works with no drag force (damping) term. These oscillations were until recently thought to coincide with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_extinction" title="Mass extinction">mass lifeform extinction</a> periods on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-extinction_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-extinction-154">[149]</a></sup> However, a reanalysis of the effects of the Sun\'s transit through the spiral structure based on CO data has failed to find a correlation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-overholt_etal_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-overholt_etal-155">[150]</a></sup>\n</p><p>It takes the Solar System about 240 million years to complete one orbit of the Milky Way (a <a href="/wiki/Galactic_year" title="Galactic year">galactic year</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sparke_gallagher_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-sparke_gallagher-14">[14]</a></sup> so the Sun is thought to have completed 18&#8211;20 orbits during its lifetime and 1/1250 of a revolution since the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Origin_of_humans" title="Origin of humans">origin of humans</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Orbital_speed" title="Orbital speed">orbital speed</a> of the Solar System about the center of the Milky Way is approximately 220&#160;km/s (490,000&#160;mph) or 0.073% of the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>. The Sun moves through the heliosphere at 84,000&#160;km/h (52,000&#160;mph).
