At this speed, it takes around 1,400 years for the Solar System to travel a distance of 1 light-year, or 8 days to travel 1 AU (<a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">astronomical unit</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garlick2002_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-garlick2002-156">[151]</a></sup> The Solar System is headed in the direction of the zodiacal constellation Scorpius, which follows the ecliptic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[152]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Galactic_rotation">Galactic rotation</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rotation_curve_(Milky_Way).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="419" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Rotation_curve_%28Milky_Way%29.JPG/200px-Rotation_curve_%28Milky_Way%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Rotation_curve_%28Milky_Way%29.JPG/300px-Rotation_curve_%28Milky_Way%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Rotation_curve_%28Milky_Way%29.JPG/400px-Rotation_curve_%28Milky_Way%29.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rotation_curve_(Milky_Way).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Galaxy_rotation_curve" title="Galaxy rotation curve">Galaxy rotation curve</a> for the Milky Way. Vertical axis is speed of rotation about the Galactic Center. Horizontal axis is distance from the Galactic Center in kpcs. The Sun is marked with a yellow ball. The observed curve of speed of rotation is blue. The predicted curve based upon stellar mass and gas in the Milky Way is red. Scatter in observations roughly indicated by gray bars. The difference is due to dark matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koupelis_Kuhn2007_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Koupelis_Kuhn2007-31">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schneider_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schneider-158">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones1_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jones1-159">[154]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The stars and gas in the Milky Way rotate about its center <a href="/wiki/Differential_rotation" title="Differential rotation">differentially</a>, meaning that the rotation period varies with location. As is typical for spiral galaxies, the orbital speed of most stars in the Milky Way does not depend strongly on their distance from the center. Away from the central bulge or outer rim, the typical stellar orbital speed is between 210&#160;&#177;&#160;10&#160;km/s (470,000&#160;&#177;&#160;22,000&#160;mph).<a class="external autonumber" href="https://arxiv.org/abs/1805.01917" rel="nofollow">[1]</a> Hence the <a href="/wiki/Orbital_period" title="Orbital period">orbital period</a> of the typical star is directly proportional only to the length of the path traveled. This is unlike the situation within the Solar System, where two-body gravitational dynamics dominate, and different orbits have significantly different velocities associated with them. The rotation curve (shown in the figure) describes this rotation. Toward the center of the Milky Way the orbit speeds are too low, whereas beyond 7 kpcs the speeds are too high to match what would be expected from the universal law of gravitation.\n</p><p>If the Milky Way contained only the mass observed in stars, gas, and other baryonic (ordinary) matter, the rotation speed would decrease with distance from the center. However, the observed curve is relatively flat, indicating that there is additional mass that cannot be detected directly with electromagnetic radiation. This inconsistency is attributed to dark matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koupelis_Kuhn2007_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-Koupelis_Kuhn2007-31">[30]</a></sup> The rotation curve of the Milky Way agrees with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universal_rotation_curve" title="Universal rotation curve">universal rotation curve</a> of spiral galaxies, the best evidence for the existence of <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a> in galaxies. Alternatively, a minority of astronomers propose that a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MOND" title="MOND">modification of the law of gravity</a> may explain the observed rotation curve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schneider_p413_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schneider_p413-160">[155]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">Formation</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_formation_and_evolution" title="Galaxy formation and evolution">Galaxy formation and evolution</a></div>\n<p>The Milky Way began as one or several small overdensities in the mass distribution in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">Universe</a> shortly after the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PHYS-20170727_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-PHYS-20170727-161">[156]</a></sup> Some of these overdensities were the seeds of globular clusters in which the oldest remaining stars in what is now the Milky Way formed. Nearly half the matter in the Milky Way may have come from other distant galaxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PHYS-20170727_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-PHYS-20170727-161">[156]</a></sup> Nonetheless, these stars and clusters now comprise the stellar halo of the Milky Way. Within a few billion years of the birth of the first stars, the mass of the Milky Way was large enough so that it was spinning relatively quickly. Due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservation_of_angular_momentum" title="Conservation of angular momentum">conservation of angular momentum</a>, this led the gaseous interstellar medium to collapse from a roughly spheroidal shape to a disk. Therefore, later generations of stars formed in this spiral disk. Most younger stars, including the Sun, are observed to be in the disk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ut20090527_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-ut20090527-162">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buser_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buser-163">[158]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Since the first stars began to form, the Milky Way has grown through both <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_merger" title="Galaxy merger">galaxy mergers</a> (particularly early in the Milky Way\'s growth) and accretion of gas directly from the Galactic halo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buser_163-1"><a href="#cite_note-Buser-163">[158]</a></sup> The Milky Way is currently accreting material from several small galaxies, including two of its largest satellite galaxies, the <a href="/wiki/Large_Magellanic_Cloud" title="Large Magellanic Cloud">Large</a> and <a href="/wiki/Small_Magellanic_Cloud" title="Small Magellanic Cloud">Small</a> Magellanic Clouds, through the <a href="/wiki/Magellanic_Stream" title="Magellanic Stream">Magellanic Stream</a>. Direct accretion of gas is observed in <a href="/wiki/High-velocity_cloud" title="High-velocity cloud">high-velocity clouds</a> like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smith_Cloud" title="Smith Cloud">Smith Cloud</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-araa35_217_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-araa35_217-164">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apj679_L21_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-apj679_L21-165">[160]</a></sup> However, properties of the Milky Way such as stellar mass, <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum" title="Angular momentum">angular momentum</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">metallicity</a> in its outermost regions suggest it has undergone no mergers with large galaxies in the last 10 billion years. This lack of recent major mergers is unusual among similar spiral galaxies; its neighbour the Andromeda Galaxy appears to have a more typical history shaped by more recent mergers with relatively large galaxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[162]</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to recent studies, the Milky Way as well as the Andromeda Galaxy lie in what in the <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_color%E2%80%93magnitude_diagram" title="Galaxy color&#8211;magnitude diagram">galaxy color&#8211;magnitude diagram</a> is known as the "green valley", a region populated by galaxies in transition from the "blue cloud" (galaxies actively forming new stars) to the "red sequence" (galaxies that lack star formation). Star-formation activity in green valley galaxies is slowing as they run out of star-forming gas in the interstellar medium. In simulated galaxies with similar properties, star formation will typically have been extinguished within about five billion years from now, even accounting for the expected, short-term increase in the rate of star formation due to the collision between both the Milky Way and the Andromeda Galaxy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[163]</a></sup> In fact, measurements of other galaxies similar to the Milky Way suggest it is among the reddest and brightest spiral galaxies that are still forming new stars and it is just slightly bluer than the bluest red sequence galaxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[164]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Age_and_cosmological_history">Age and cosmological history</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_firestorm_of_star_birth_(artist%E2%80%99s_illustration).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="84" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/A_firestorm_of_star_birth_%28artist%E2%80%99s_illustration%29.jpg/150px-A_firestorm_of_star_birth_%28artist%E2%80%99s_illustration%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/A_firestorm_of_star_birth_%28artist%E2%80%99s_illustration%29.jpg/225px-A_firestorm_of_star_birth_%28artist%E2%80%99s_illustration%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/A_firestorm_of_star_birth_%28artist%E2%80%99s_illustration%29.jpg/300px-A_firestorm_of_star_birth_%28artist%E2%80%99s_illustration%29.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_firestorm_of_star_birth_(artist%E2%80%99s_illustration).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Night sky from a hypothetical planet within the Milky Way 10 billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[165]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Globular clusters are among the oldest objects in the Milky Way, which thus set a lower limit on the age of the Milky Way. The ages of individual stars in the Milky Way can be estimated by measuring the abundance of long-lived <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactive_element" title="Radioactive element">radioactive elements</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thorium-232" title="Thorium-232">thorium-232</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uranium-238" title="Uranium-238">uranium-238</a>, then comparing the results to estimates of their original abundance, a technique called <a href="/wiki/Nucleocosmochronology" title="Nucleocosmochronology">nucleocosmochronology</a>. These yield values of about <span class="nowrap">12.5 &#177; 3 billion years</span> for <a href="/wiki/Cayrel%27s_Star" title="Cayrel\'s Star">CS 31082-001</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[166]</a></sup> and <span class="nowrap">13.8 &#177; 4 billion years</span> for <a href="/wiki/BD_%2B17%C2%B0_3248" title="BD +17&#176; 3248">BD +17&#176; 3248</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[167]</a></sup> Once a <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarf</a> is formed, it begins to undergo radiative cooling and the surface temperature steadily drops. By measuring the temperatures of the coolest of these white dwarfs and comparing them to their expected initial temperature, an age estimate can be made. With this technique, the age of the globular cluster M4 was estimated as <span class="nowrap">12.7 &#177; 0.7 billion years</span>. Age estimates of the oldest of these clusters gives a best fit estimate of 12.6&#160;billion years, and a 95% confidence upper limit of 16&#160;billion years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science299_5603_65_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-science299_5603_65-173">[168]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Several individual stars have been found in the Milky Way\'s halo with measured ages very close to the 13.80-billion-year <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Universe" title="Age of the Universe">age of the Universe</a>. In 2007, a star in the galactic halo, <a href="/wiki/HE_1523-0901" title="HE 1523-0901">HE 1523-0901</a>, was estimated to be about 13.2 billion years old. As the oldest known object in the Milky Way at that time, this measurement placed a lower limit on the age of the Milky Way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frebel_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-frebel-174">[169]</a></sup> This estimate was made using the UV-Visual Echelle Spectrograph of the <a href="/wiki/Very_Large_Telescope" title="Very Large Telescope">Very Large Telescope</a> to <a href="/wiki/Measurement" title="Measurement">measure</a> the relative strengths of <a href="/wiki/Spectral_line" title="Spectral line">spectral lines</a> caused by the presence of <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">elements</a> created by the <a href="/wiki/R-process" title="R-process">R-process</a>. The line strengths yield abundances of different elemental <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a>, from which an estimate of the age of the star can be derived using <a href="/wiki/Nucleocosmochronology" title="Nucleocosmochronology">nucleocosmochronology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frebel_174-1"><a href="#cite_note-frebel-174">[169]</a></sup> Another star, <a href="/wiki/HD_140283" title="HD 140283">HD 140283</a>, is 14.5 &#177; 0.7 billion years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HD_140283_(arXiv)_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-HD_140283_(arXiv)-32">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[170]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The age of stars in the galactic <a href="/wiki/Thin_disk" title="Thin disk">thin disk</a> has also been estimated using nucleocosmochronology. Measurements of thin disk stars yield an estimate that the thin disk formed 8.8 &#177; 1.7 billion years ago. These measurements suggest there was a hiatus of almost 5 billion years between the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Galactic_halo" title="Galactic halo">galactic halo</a> and the thin disk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-del_Peloso_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-del_Peloso-176">[171]</a></sup> Recent analysis of the chemical signatures of thousands of stars suggests that stellar formation might have dropped by an order of magnitude at the time of disk formation, 10 to 8 billion years ago, when interstellar gas was too hot to form new stars at the same rate as before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[172]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The satellite galaxies surrounding the Milky way are not randomly distributed but seemed to be the result of a break-up of some larger system producing a ring structure 500,000 light years in diameter and 50,000 light-years wide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[173]</a></sup> Close encounters between galaxies, like that expected in 4 billion years with the Andromeda Galaxy rips off huge tails of gas, which, over time can coalesce to form dwarf galaxies in a ring at an arbitrary angle to the main disc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[174]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In November 2018, astronomers reported the discovery of one of the oldest stars in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>. About 13.5 billion-years-old, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2MASS_J18082002-5104378_B" title="2MASS J18082002-5104378 B">2MASS J18082002-5104378 B</a> is a tiny ultra metal-poor (UMP) star made almost entirely of materials released from the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>, and is possibly one of the very first stars. The discovery of the star in the Milky Way <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxy</a> suggests that the galaxy may be at least 3 billion years older than previously thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EA-20181105_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-EA-20181105-180">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JHU-20181105_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-JHU-20181105-181">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ApJ-20181105_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-ApJ-20181105-182">[177]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environment">Environment</span></h2>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:06-Local_Group_(LofE06240).png"><img alt="" data-file-height="3786" data-file-width="5172" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/06-Local_Group_%28LofE06240%29.png/220px-06-Local_Group_%28LofE06240%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/06-Local_Group_%28LofE06240%29.png/330px-06-Local_Group_%28LofE06240%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/06-Local_Group_%28LofE06240%29.png/440px-06-Local_Group_%28LofE06240%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Diagram of the galaxies in the <a href="/wiki/Local_Group" title="Local Group">Local Group</a> relative to the Milky Way</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:07-Laniakea_(LofE07240).png"><img alt="" data-file-height="3786" data-file-width="5172" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/07-Laniakea_%28LofE07240%29.png/220px-07-Laniakea_%28LofE07240%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/07-Laniakea_%28LofE07240%29.png/330px-07-Laniakea_%28LofE07240%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/07-Laniakea_%28LofE07240%29.png/440px-07-Laniakea_%28LofE07240%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The position of the Local Group within the <a href="/wiki/Laniakea_Supercluster" title="Laniakea Supercluster">Laniakea Supercluster</a></div></div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Local_Group" title="Local Group">Local Group</a></div>\n<p>The Milky Way and the <a href="/wiki/Andromeda_Galaxy" title="Andromeda Galaxy">Andromeda Galaxy</a> are a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Binary_system_(astronomy)" title="Binary system (astronomy)">binary system</a> of giant spiral galaxies belonging to a group of 50 closely bound galaxies known as the <a href="/wiki/Local_Group" title="Local Group">Local Group</a>, surrounded by a Local Void, itself being part of the <a href="/wiki/Virgo_Supercluster" title="Virgo Supercluster">Virgo Supercluster</a>. Surrounding the Virgo Supercluster are a number of voids, devoid of many galaxies, the Microscopium Void to the "north", the Sculptor Void to the "left", the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bootes_Void" title="Bootes Void">Bootes Void</a> to the "right" and the Canes-Major Void to the South. These voids change shape over time, creating filamentous structures of galaxies. The Virgo Supercluster, for instance, is being drawn towards the <a href="/wiki/Great_Attractor" title="Great Attractor">Great Attractor</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[178]</a></sup> which in turn forms part of a greater structure, called <a href="/wiki/Laniakea_Supercluster" title="Laniakea Supercluster">Laniakea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[179]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Two smaller galaxies and a number of <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_galaxy" title="Dwarf galaxy">dwarf galaxies</a> in the Local Group orbit the Milky Way. The largest of these is the <a href="/wiki/Large_Magellanic_Cloud" title="Large Magellanic Cloud">Large Magellanic Cloud</a> with a diameter of 14,000 light-years. It has a close companion, the <a href="/wiki/Small_Magellanic_Cloud" title="Small Magellanic Cloud">Small Magellanic Cloud</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Magellanic_Stream" title="Magellanic Stream">Magellanic Stream</a> is a stream of neutral <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> gas extending from these two small galaxies across 100&#176; of the sky. The stream is thought to have been dragged from the Magellanic Clouds in tidal interactions with the Milky Way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[180]</a></sup> Some of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milky_Way%27s_satellite_galaxies" title="Milky Way\'s satellite galaxies">dwarf galaxies orbiting the Milky Way</a> are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canis_Major_Dwarf" title="Canis Major Dwarf">Canis Major Dwarf</a> (the closest), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sagittarius_Dwarf_Elliptical_Galaxy" title="Sagittarius Dwarf Elliptical Galaxy">Sagittarius Dwarf Elliptical Galaxy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ursa_Minor_Dwarf" title="Ursa Minor Dwarf">Ursa Minor Dwarf</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sculptor_Dwarf" title="Sculptor Dwarf">Sculptor Dwarf</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sextans_Dwarf" title="Sextans Dwarf">Sextans Dwarf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fornax_Dwarf" title="Fornax Dwarf">Fornax Dwarf</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leo_I_Dwarf" title="Leo I Dwarf">Leo I Dwarf</a>. The smallest dwarf galaxies of the Milky Way are only 500 light-years in diameter. These include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carina_Dwarf" title="Carina Dwarf">Carina Dwarf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Draco_Dwarf" title="Draco Dwarf">Draco Dwarf</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Leo_II_(dwarf_galaxy)" title="Leo II (dwarf galaxy)">Leo II Dwarf</a>. There may still be undetected dwarf galaxies that are dynamically bound to the Milky Way, which is supported by the detection of nine new satellites of the Milky Way in a relatively small patch of the night sky in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koposov2015_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koposov2015-186">[181]</a></sup> There are also some dwarf galaxies that have already been absorbed by the Milky Way, such as the progenitor of <a href="/wiki/Omega_Centauri" title="Omega Centauri">Omega Centauri</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[182]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2014 researchers reported that most satellite galaxies of the Milky Way actually lie in a very large disk and orbit in the same direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[183]</a></sup> This came as a surprise: according to standard cosmology, the satellite galaxies should form in dark matter halos, and they should be widely distributed and moving in random directions. This discrepancy is still not fully explained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[184]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In January 2006, researchers reported that the heretofore unexplained warp in the disk of the Milky Way has now been mapped and found to be a ripple or vibration set up by the Large and Small Magellanic Clouds as they orbit the Milky Way, causing vibrations when they pass through its edges. Previously, these two galaxies, at around 2% of the mass of the Milky Way, were considered too small to influence the Milky Way. However, in a computer model, the movement of these two galaxies creates a dark matter wake that amplifies their influence on the larger Milky Way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berkeley20060109_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-berkeley20060109-190">[185]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Current measurements suggest the Andromeda Galaxy is approaching us at 100 to 140&#160;km/s (220,000 to 310,000&#160;mph). In 3 to 4 billion years, there may be an <a href="/wiki/Andromeda%E2%80%93Milky_Way_collision" title="Andromeda&#8211;Milky Way collision">Andromeda&#8211;Milky Way collision</a>, depending on the importance of unknown lateral components to the galaxies\' relative motion. If they collide, the chance of individual <a href="/wiki/Stellar_collision" title="Stellar collision">stars colliding</a> with each other is extremely low, but instead the two galaxies will merge to form a single <a href="/wiki/Elliptical_galaxy" title="Elliptical galaxy">elliptical galaxy</a> or perhaps a large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disk_galaxy" title="Disk galaxy">disk galaxy</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ueda2014_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ueda2014-191">[186]</a></sup> over the course of about a billion years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wong20000414_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-wong20000414-192">[187]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Velocity">Velocity</span></h2>\n<p>Although <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">special relativity</a> states that there is no "preferred" <a href="/wiki/Inertial_frame_of_reference" title="Inertial frame of reference">inertial frame of reference</a> in space with which to compare the Milky Way, the Milky Way does have a velocity with respect to cosmological <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frames_of_reference" title="Frames of reference">frames of reference</a>.\n</p><p>One such frame of reference is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hubble_flow" title="Hubble flow">Hubble flow</a>, the apparent motions of galaxy clusters due to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Expansion_of_space" title="Expansion of space">expansion of space</a>. Individual galaxies, including the Milky Way, have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peculiar_velocities" title="Peculiar velocities">peculiar velocities</a> relative to the average flow. Thus, to compare the Milky Way to the Hubble flow, one must consider a volume large enough so that the expansion of the Universe dominates over local, random motions. A large enough volume means that the mean motion of galaxies within this volume is equal to the Hubble flow. Astronomers believe the Milky Way is moving at approximately 630&#160;km/s (1,400,000&#160;mph) with respect to this local co-moving frame of reference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones2_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2-193">[188]</a></sup> The Milky Way is moving in the general direction of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Attractor" title="Great Attractor">Great Attractor</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_cluster" title="Galaxy cluster">galaxy clusters</a>, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shapley_supercluster" title="Shapley supercluster">Shapley supercluster</a>, behind it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[189]</a></sup> The Local Group (a cluster of gravitationally bound galaxies containing, among others, the Milky Way and the Andromeda Galaxy) is part of a <a href="/wiki/Supercluster" title="Supercluster">supercluster</a> called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Local_Supercluster" title="Local Supercluster">Local Supercluster</a>, centered near the <a href="/wiki/Virgo_Cluster" title="Virgo Cluster">Virgo Cluster</a>: although they are moving away from each other at 967&#160;km/s (2,160,000&#160;mph) as part of the Hubble flow, this velocity is less than would be expected given the 16.8&#160;million pc distance due to the gravitational attraction between the Local Group and the Virgo Cluster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[190]</a></sup>\n</p><p><span id="cmb"></span>Another reference frame is provided by the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background" title="Cosmic microwave background">cosmic microwave background</a> (CMB). The Milky Way is moving at <span class="nowrap">552&#160;&#177;&#160;6&#160;km/s (1,235,000&#160;&#177;&#160;13,000&#160;mph)</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dipole_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-dipole-196">[191]</a></sup> with respect to the photons of the CMB, toward 10.5 right ascension, &#8722;24&#176; declination (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J2000" title="J2000">J2000</a> epoch, near the center of <a href="/wiki/Hydra_(constellation)" title="Hydra (constellation)">Hydra</a>). This motion is observed by satellites such as the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_Background_Explorer" title="Cosmic Background Explorer">Cosmic Background Explorer</a> (COBE) and the <a href="/wiki/Wilkinson_Microwave_Anisotropy_Probe" title="Wilkinson Microwave Anisotropy Probe">Wilkinson Microwave Anisotropy Probe</a> (WMAP) as a dipole contribution to the CMB, as photons in equilibrium in the CMB frame get <a href="/wiki/Doppler_effect" title="Doppler effect">blue-shifted</a> in the direction of the motion and <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">red-shifted</a> in the opposite direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dipole_196-1"><a href="#cite_note-dipole-196">[191]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_mythology">Etymology and mythology</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jacopo_Tintoretto_-_The_Origin_of_the_Milky_Way_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2684" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Jacopo_Tintoretto_-_The_Origin_of_the_Milky_Way_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/290px-Jacopo_Tintoretto_-_The_Origin_of_the_Milky_Way_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Jacopo_Tintoretto_-_The_Origin_of_the_Milky_Way_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/435px-Jacopo_Tintoretto_-_The_Origin_of_the_Milky_Way_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Jacopo_Tintoretto_-_The_Origin_of_the_Milky_Way_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/580px-Jacopo_Tintoretto_-_The_Origin_of_the_Milky_Way_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jacopo_Tintoretto_-_The_Origin_of_the_Milky_Way_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/The_Origin_of_the_Milky_Way_(Tintoretto)" title="The Origin of the Milky Way (Tintoretto)">The Origin of the Milky Way</a></i> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1575&#8211;1580) by <a href="/wiki/Tintoretto" title="Tintoretto">Tintoretto</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/List_of_names_for_the_Milky_Way" title="List of names for the Milky Way">List of names for the Milky Way</a> and <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way_(mythology)" title="Milky Way (mythology)">Milky Way (mythology)</a></div>\n<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonian</a> epic poem <i><a href="/wiki/En%C3%BBma_Eli%C5%A1" title="En&#251;ma Eli&#353;">En&#251;ma Eli&#353;</a></i>, the Milky Way is created from the severed tail of the primeval salt water <a href="/wiki/Dragon" title="Dragon">dragoness</a> <a href="/wiki/Tiamat" title="Tiamat">Tiamat</a>, set in the sky by <a href="/wiki/Marduk" title="Marduk">Marduk</a>, the Babylonian <a href="/wiki/National_god" title="National god">national god</a>, after slaying her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[192]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[193]</a></sup> This story was once thought to have been based on an older <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerian</a> version in which Tiamat is instead slain by <a href="/wiki/Enlil" title="Enlil">Enlil</a> of <a href="/wiki/Nippur" title="Nippur">Nippur</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[194]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lambert1964_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lambert1964-200">[195]</a></sup> but is now thought to be purely an invention of Babylonian propagandists with the intention to show Marduk as superior to the Sumerian deities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lambert1964_200-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lambert1964-200">[195]</a></sup>\n</p><p><b>Llys D&#244;n</b> (literally "The Court of <a href="/wiki/D%C3%B4n" title="D&#244;n">D&#244;n</a>") is the traditional <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a> name for the constellation <a href="/wiki/Cassiopeia_(constellation)" title="Cassiopeia (constellation)">Cassiopeia</a>. At least three of D&#244;n\'s children also have astronomical associations: <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Caer_Gwydion&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Caer Gwydion (page does not exist)">Caer Gwydion</a> ("The fortress of <a href="/wiki/Gwydion" title="Gwydion">Gwydion</a>") is the traditional Welsh name for the Milky Way, and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Caer_Arianrhod&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Caer Arianrhod (page does not exist)">Caer Arianrhod</a> ("The Fortress of <a href="/wiki/Arianrhod" title="Arianrhod">Arianrhod</a>") being the constellation of <a href="/wiki/Corona_Borealis" title="Corona Borealis">Corona Borealis</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>In western culture, the name "Milky Way" is derived from its appearance as a dim un-resolved "milky" glowing band arching across the night sky. The term is a translation of the <a href="/wiki/Classical_Latin" title="Classical Latin">Classical Latin</a> <i>via lactea</i>, in turn derived from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic_Greek" title="Hellenistic Greek">Hellenistic Greek</a> <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#947;&#945;&#955;&#945;&#958;&#943;&#945;&#962;</span>, short for <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#947;&#945;&#955;&#945;&#958;&#943;&#945;&#962; &#954;&#973;&#954;&#955;&#959;&#962;</span> (<i><i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek-language transliteration">galax&#237;as k&#253;klos</i></i>, "milky circle"). The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#947;&#945;&#955;&#945;&#958;&#943;&#945;&#962;</span> (<i><i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek-language transliteration">galaxias</i></i>) &#8211; from root <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#947;&#945;&#955;&#945;&#954;&#964;</span>-, <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#947;&#940;&#955;&#945;</span> ("milk") + <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">-&#943;&#945;&#962;</span> (forming adjectives) &#8211; is also the root of "galaxy", the name for our, and later all such, collections of stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eo_galaxy_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-eo_galaxy-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jankowski2010_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-jankowski2010-201">[196]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schiller2010_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-schiller2010-202">[197]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxford_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-oxford-203">[198]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>, the Milky Way was formed after the <a href="/wiki/Trickster" title="Trickster">trickster</a> god <a href="/wiki/Hermes" title="Hermes">Hermes</a> suckled the infant <a href="/wiki/Heracles" title="Heracles">Heracles</a> at the breast of <a href="/wiki/Hera" title="Hera">Hera</a>, the queen of the gods, while she was asleep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leeming_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leeming-204">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pache_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pache-205">[200]</a></sup> When Hera awoke, she tore Heracles away from her breast and splattered her breast milk across the heavens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leeming_204-1"><a href="#cite_note-Leeming-204">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pache_205-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pache-205">[200]</a></sup> In another version of the story, <a href="/wiki/Athena" title="Athena">Athena</a>, the patron goddess of heroes, tricked Hera into suckling Heracles voluntarily,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leeming_204-2"><a href="#cite_note-Leeming-204">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pache_205-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pache-205">[200]</a></sup> but he bit her nipple so hard that she flung him away, spraying milk everywhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leeming_204-3"><a href="#cite_note-Leeming-204">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pache_205-3"><a href="#cite_note-Pache-205">[200]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Milky Way, or "milk circle", was just one of 11 "circles" the Greeks identified in the sky, others being the <a href="/wiki/Zodiac" title="Zodiac">zodiac</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Meridian_(astronomy)" title="Meridian (astronomy)">meridian</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Horizon" title="Horizon">horizon</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tropics_of_Cancer_and_Capricorn" title="Tropics of Cancer and Capricorn">tropics of Cancer and Capricorn</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arctic_circle" title="Arctic circle">Arctic</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antarctic_circle" title="Antarctic circle">Antarctic circles</a>, and two <a href="/wiki/Colure" title="Colure">colure</a> circles passing through both poles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eratosthenes1997_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-eratosthenes1997-206">[201]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomical_history">Astronomical history</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Galaxy#Observation_history" title="Galaxy">Galaxy &#167;&#160;Observation history</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Herschel-Galaxy.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="668" decoding="async" height="66" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Herschel-Galaxy.png/220px-Herschel-Galaxy.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Herschel-Galaxy.png/330px-Herschel-Galaxy.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Herschel-Galaxy.png/440px-Herschel-Galaxy.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Herschel-Galaxy.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The shape of the Milky Way as deduced from star counts by <a href="/wiki/William_Herschel" title="William Herschel">William Herschel</a> in 1785; the Solar System was assumed near center</div></div></div>\n<p>In <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meteorologica" title="Meteorologica">Meteorologica</a></i> (DK 59 A80), <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> (384&#8211;322 BC) wrote that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_philosophy" title="Greek philosophy">Greek philosophers</a> <a href="/wiki/Anaxagoras" title="Anaxagoras">Anaxagoras</a> (c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;500</span>&#8211;428 BC) and <a href="/wiki/Democritus" title="Democritus">Democritus</a> (460&#8211;370 BC) proposed that the Milky Way might consist of distant <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[202]</a></sup> However, Aristotle himself believed the Milky Way to be caused by "the ignition of the fiery exhalation of some stars which were large, numerous and close together"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[203]</a></sup> and that the "ignition takes place in the upper part of the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>, in the region of the world which is continuous with the heavenly motions."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Montada_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-Montada-210">[205]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neoplatonist</a> philosopher <a href="/wiki/Olympiodorus_the_Younger" title="Olympiodorus the Younger">Olympiodorus the Younger</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;495</span>&#8211;570 <small>A.D.</small>) criticized this view, arguing that if the Milky Way were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sublunary" title="Sublunary">sublunary</a>, it should appear different at different times and places on Earth, and that it should have <a href="/wiki/Parallax" title="Parallax">parallax</a>, which it does not. In his view, the Milky Way is celestial. This idea would be influential later in the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Islamic world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heidarzadeh2008_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-heidarzadeh2008-211">[206]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persian</a> astronomer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ab%C5%AB_Rayh%C4%81n_al-B%C4%ABr%C5%ABn%C4%AB" title="Ab&#363; Rayh&#257;n al-B&#299;r&#363;n&#299;">Ab&#363; Rayh&#257;n al-B&#299;r&#363;n&#299;</a> (973&#8211;1048) proposed that the Milky Way is "a collection of countless fragments of the nature of <a href="/wiki/Nebula" title="Nebula">nebulous</a> stars".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mactutor_albirundi_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-mactutor_albirundi-212">[207]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Andalusian</a> astronomer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibn_Bajjah" title="Ibn Bajjah">Avempace</a> (<abbr title="died">d</abbr> 1138) proposed the Milky Way to be made up of many stars but appears to be a continuous image due to the effect of <a href="/wiki/Refraction" title="Refraction">refraction</a> in <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">Earth\'s atmosphere</a>, citing his observation of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conjunction_(astronomy_and_astrology)" title="Conjunction (astronomy and astrology)">conjunction</a> of Jupiter and Mars in 1106 or 1107 as evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Montada_210-1"><a href="#cite_note-Montada-210">[205]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibn_Qayyim_Al-Jawziyya" title="Ibn Qayyim Al-Jawziyya">Ibn Qayyim Al-Jawziyya</a> (1292&#8211;1350) proposed that the Milky Way is "a myriad of tiny stars packed together in the sphere of the fixed stars" and that these stars are larger than <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Livingston_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-Livingston-213">[208]</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to Jamil Ragep, the Persian astronomer <a href="/wiki/Nasir_al-Din_al-Tusi" title="Nasir al-Din al-Tusi">Na&#7779;&#299;r al-D&#299;n al-&#7788;&#363;s&#299;</a> (1201&#8211;1274) in his <i>Tadhkira</i> writes:\n"The Milky Way, i.e. the Galaxy, is made up of a very large number of small, tightly clustered stars, which, on account of their concentration and smallness, seem to be cloudy patches. Because of this, it was likened to milk in color."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ragep1993_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-ragep1993-214">[209]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Actual proof of the Milky Way consisting of many stars came in 1610 when <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a> used a <a href="/wiki/Optical_telescope" title="Optical telescope">telescope</a> to study the Milky Way and discovered that it is composed of a huge number of faint stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[210]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oconnor_robertson2002_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-oconnor_robertson2002-216">[211]</a></sup> In a treatise in 1755, <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a>, drawing on earlier work by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Wright_(astronomer)" title="Thomas Wright (astronomer)">Thomas Wright</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[212]</a></sup> speculated (correctly) that the Milky Way might be a rotating body of a huge number of stars, held together by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitation" title="Gravitation">gravitational forces</a> akin to the Solar System but on much larger scales.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[213]</a></sup> The resulting disk of stars would be seen as a band on the sky from our perspective inside the disk. Kant also conjectured that some of the <a href="/wiki/Nebula" title="Nebula">nebulae</a> visible in the night sky might be separate "galaxies" themselves, similar to our own. Kant referred to both the Milky Way and the "extragalactic nebulae" as "island universes", a term still current up to the 1930s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[214]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-our_galaxy_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-our_galaxy-220">[215]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[216]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first attempt to describe the shape of the Milky Way and the position of the Sun within it was carried out by <a href="/wiki/William_Herschel" title="William Herschel">William Herschel</a> in 1785 by carefully counting the number of stars in different regions of the visible sky. He produced a diagram of the shape of the Milky Way with the Solar System close to the center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[217]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1845, <a href="/wiki/William_Parsons,_3rd_Earl_of_Rosse" title="William Parsons, 3rd Earl of Rosse">Lord Rosse</a> constructed a new telescope and was able to distinguish between elliptical and spiral-shaped nebulae. He also managed to make out individual point sources in some of these nebulae, lending credence to Kant\'s earlier conjecture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abbey_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-abbey-223">[218]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pic_iroberts1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="371" data-file-width="569" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Pic_iroberts1.jpg/220px-Pic_iroberts1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Pic_iroberts1.jpg/330px-Pic_iroberts1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Pic_iroberts1.jpg/440px-Pic_iroberts1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pic_iroberts1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Photograph of the "Great Andromeda Nebula" from 1899, later identified as the <a href="/wiki/Andromeda_Galaxy" title="Andromeda Galaxy">Andromeda Galaxy</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1904, studying the <a href="/wiki/Proper_motion" title="Proper motion">proper motions</a> of stars, <a href="/wiki/Jacobus_Kapteyn" title="Jacobus Kapteyn">Jacobus Kapteyn</a> reported that these were not random, as it was believed in that time; stars could be divided into two streams, moving in nearly opposite directions. It was later realized that Kapteyn\'s data had been the first evidence of the rotation of our Galaxy, which ultimately led to the finding of galactic rotation by <a href="/wiki/Bertil_Lindblad" title="Bertil Lindblad">Bertil Lindblad</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jan_Oort" title="Jan Oort">Jan Oort</a>.\n</p><p>In 1917, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heber_Curtis" title="Heber Curtis">Heber Curtis</a> had observed the nova <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S_Andromedae" title="S Andromedae">S Andromedae</a> within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Andromeda_Nebula" title="Great Andromeda Nebula">Great Andromeda Nebula</a> (<a href="/wiki/Messier_object" title="Messier object">Messier object</a> 31). Searching the photographic record, he found 11 more <a href="/wiki/Nova" title="Nova">novae</a>. Curtis noticed that these novae were, on average, 10 <a href="/wiki/Magnitude_(astronomy)" title="Magnitude (astronomy)">magnitudes</a> fainter than those that occurred within the Milky Way. As a result, he was able to come up with a distance estimate of 150,000 parsecs. He became a proponent of the "island universes" hypothesis, which held that the spiral nebulae were actually independent galaxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pasp100_6_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-pasp100_6-224">[219]</a></sup> In 1920 the <a href="/wiki/Great_Debate_(astronomy)" title="Great Debate (astronomy)">Great Debate</a> took place between <a href="/wiki/Harlow_Shapley" title="Harlow Shapley">Harlow Shapley</a> and Heber Curtis, concerning the nature of the Milky Way, spiral nebulae, and the dimensions of the Universe. To support his claim that the Great Andromeda Nebula is an external galaxy, Curtis noted the appearance of dark lanes resembling the dust clouds in the Milky Way, as well as the significant <a href="/wiki/Doppler_effect" title="Doppler effect">Doppler shift</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weaver_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-weaver-225">[220]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The controversy was conclusively settled by <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Hubble" title="Edwin Hubble">Edwin Hubble</a> in the early 1920s using the Mount Wilson observatory <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hooker_telescope" title="Hooker telescope">2.5&#160;m (100&#160;in) Hooker telescope</a>. With the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light-gathering_power" title="Light-gathering power">light-gathering power</a> of this new telescope, he was able to produce <a href="/wiki/Astrophotography" title="Astrophotography">astronomical photographs</a> that resolved the outer parts of some spiral nebulae as collections of individual stars. He was also able to identify some <a href="/wiki/Cepheid_variable" title="Cepheid variable">Cepheid variables</a> that he could use as a <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/benchmark" title="wikt:benchmark">benchmark</a> to estimate the distance to the nebulae. He found that the Andromeda Nebula is 275,000 parsecs from the Sun, far too distant to be part of the Milky Way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jrasc83_6_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-jrasc83_6-226">[221]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apj69_103_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-apj69_103-227">[222]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mapping">Mapping</span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">ESA</a> spacecraft <i><a href="/wiki/Gaia_(spacecraft)" title="Gaia (spacecraft)">Gaia</a></i> provides distance estimates by determining the <a href="/wiki/Parallax" title="Parallax">parallax</a> of a billion stars and is mapping the Milky Way with four planned releases of maps in 2022.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[223]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[224]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>\n<ul><li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Portal-puzzle.svg"><img alt="Portal-puzzle.svg" class="noviewer" data-file-height="28" data-file-width="32" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/Portal-puzzle.svg/16px-Portal-puzzle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/Portal-puzzle.svg/24px-Portal-puzzle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/Portal-puzzle.svg/32px-Portal-puzzle.svg.png 2x" width="16"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Milky_Way" title="Portal:Milky Way">Milky Way portal</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Baade%27s_Window" title="Baade\'s Window">Baade\'s Window</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Oort_constants" title="Oort constants">Oort constants</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-context-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-context_17-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Jay M. Pasachoff in his textbook <i>Astronomy: From the Earth to the Universe</i> states the term <i>Milky Way</i> should refer exclusively to the band of light that the galaxy forms in the <a href="/wiki/Night_sky" title="Night sky">night sky</a>, while the galaxy should receive the full name <i>Milky Way Galaxy</i>; however, this does not reflect a firm consensus in the astronomical community. See:\n<ul><li><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Jay_Pasachoff" title="Jay Pasachoff">Pasachoff, Jay M.</a> (1994). <i>Astronomy: From the Earth to the Universe</i>. Harcourt School. p.&#160;500. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-03-001667-7" class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">These estimates are very uncertain, as most non-star objects are hard to detect; for example, black hole estimates range from ten million to one billion.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-chandra2003-102"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-chandra2003_102-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For a photo see: <cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://chandra.harvard.edu/photo/2003/0203long/" rel="nofollow">"Sagittarius A*: Milky Way monster stars in cosmic reality show"</a>. <i>Chandra X-ray Observatory</i>. Harvard-Smithsonian Center for Astrophysics. January 6, 2003. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20080317061809/http://chandra.harvard.edu/photo/2003/0203long/" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on March 17, 2008<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">May 20,</span> 2012</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Chandra+X-ray+Observatory&amp;rft.atitle=Sagittarius+A%2A%3A+Milky+Way+monster+stars+in+cosmic+reality+show&amp;rft.date=2003-01-06&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fchandra.harvard.edu%2Fphoto%2F2003%2F0203long%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMilky+Way"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>\n</li>\n</ol></div>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-milky_way_mag-73"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-milky_way_mag_73-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Karachentsev et al. give a <i>blue</i> absolute magnitude of &#8722;20.8. Combined with a <a href="/wiki/Color_index" title="Color index">color index</a> of 0.55 estimated <a class="external text" href="http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2014MNRAS.440..405M" rel="nofollow">here</a>, an absolute visual magnitude of &#8722;21.35 (&#8722;20.8 &#8722; 0.55 = &#8722;21.35) is obtained. Note that determining the absolute magnitude of the Milky Way is very difficult, because Earth is inside it.</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-ssr100_1_129-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-ssr100_1_129_1-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ssr100_1_129_1-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Gerhard, O. href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-42">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The bright center of the galaxy is located in the <a href="/wiki/Constellation" title="Constellation">constellation</a> <a href="/wiki/Sagittarius_(constellation)" title="Sagittarius (constellation)">Sagittarius</a>. From Sagittarius, the hazy band of white light appears to pass westward through the constellations of <a href="/wiki/Scorpius" title="Scorpius">Scorpius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ara_(constellation)" title="Ara (constellation)">Ara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norma_(constellation)" title="Norma (constellation)">Norma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Triangulum_Australe" title="Triangulum Australe">Triangulum Australe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Circinus" title="Circinus">Circinus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Centaurus" title="Centaurus">Centaurus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Musca" title="Musca">Musca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crux" title="Crux">Crux</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carina_(constellation)" title="Carina (constellation)">Carina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vela_(constellation)" title="Vela (constellation)">Vela</a>, <a href="/wiki/Puppis" title="Puppis">Puppis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canis_Major" title="Canis Major">Canis Major</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monoceros" title="Monoceros">Monoceros</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orion_(constellation)" title="Orion (constellation)">Orion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gemini_(constellation)" title="Gemini (constellation)">Gemini</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taurus_(constellation)" title="Taurus (constellation)">Taurus</a>, to the <a href="/wiki/Galactic_anticenter" title="Galactic anticenter">galactic anticenter</a> in <a href="/wiki/Auriga_(constellation)" title="Auriga (constellation)">Auriga</a>. From there, it passes through <a href="/wiki/Perseus_(constellation)" title="Perseus (constellation)">Perseus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andromeda_(constellation)" title="Andromeda (constellation)">Andromeda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cassiopeia_(constellation)" title="Cassiopeia (constellation)">Cassiopeia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cepheus_(constellation)" title="Cepheus (constellation)">Cepheus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lacerta" title="Lacerta">Lacerta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cygnus_(constellation)" title="Cygnus (constellation)">Cygnus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vulpecula" title="Vulpecula">Vulpecula</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sagitta" title="Sagitta">Sagitta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aquila_(constellation)" title="Aquila (constellation)">Aquila</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ophiuchus" title="Ophiuchus">Ophiuchus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scutum_(constellation)" title="Scutum (constellation)">Scutum</a>, and back to <a href="/wiki/Sagittarius_(constellation)" title="Sagittarius (constellation)">Sagittarius</a>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-43">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.cfa.harvard.edu/seuforum/howfar/across.html" rel="nofollow">"How Big is Our Universe: How far is it across the Milky Way?"</a>. <i>NASA-Smithsonian Education Forum on the Structure and Evolution of the Universe, at the Harvard Smithsonian Center for Astrophysics</i>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20130305005817/http://www.cfa.harvard.edu/seuforum/howfar/across.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on March 5, 2013<span class="reference-accessdate">. New York: Springer-Verlag. p.&#160;129.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Nasir+al-Din+al-Tusi%27s+Memoir+on+Astronomy+%28al-Tadhkira+fi+%27ilm+al-hay%27+a%29&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pages=129&amp;rft.pub=Springer-Verlag&amp;rft.date=1993&amp;rft.aulast=Ragep&amp;rft.aufirst=Jamil&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMilky+Way"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-215"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-215">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Galileo Galilei, <i>Sidereus Nuncius</i> (Venice, (Italy):  Thomas Baglioni, 1610), <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/stream/Sidereusnuncius00Gali#page/n37/mode/2up" rel="nofollow">pages 15 and 16.</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20160316173644/https://archive.org/stream/Sidereusnuncius00Gali" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> March 16, 2016, at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a><br/>\nEnglish translation:  Galileo Galilei with Edward Stafford Carlos, trans., <i>The Sidereal Messenger</i> (London, England:  Rivingtons, 1880), <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/stream/siderealmessenge80gali#page/42/mode/2up/" rel="nofollow">pages 42 and 43.</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20121202215542/http://archive.org/stream/siderealmessenge80gali" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> December 2, 2012, at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-oconnor_robertson2002-216"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-oconnor_robertson2002_216-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">O\'Connor, J. J.; Robertson, E. F. (November 2002). <a class="external text" href="http://www-gap.dcs.st-and.ac.uk/~history/Biographies/Galileo.html" rel="nofollow">"Galileo Galilei"</a>. University of St. Andrews. <a class="external text" href="https://archive.is/20120530/http://www-gap.dcs.st-and.ac.uk/~history/Biographies/Galileo.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on May 30, 2012<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">January 8,</span> 2007</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Galileo+Galilei&amp;rft.pub=University+of+St.+Andrews&amp;rft.date=2002-11&amp;rft.aulast=O%27Connor&amp;rft.aufirst=J.+J.&amp;rft.au=Robertson%2C+E.+F.&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww-gap.dcs.st-and.ac.uk%2F~history%2FBiographies%2FGalileo.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMilky+Way"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-217"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-217">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Thomas Wright, <i>An Original Theory or New Hypothesis of the Universe</i> &#8230; (London, England:  H. Chapelle, 1750).\n<ul><li><a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=80VZAAAAcAAJ&amp;pg=PA57" rel="nofollow">On page 57</a>, Wright stated that despite their mutual gravitational attraction, the stars in the constellations don\'t collide because they are in orbit, so centrifugal force keeps them separated:  " &#8230; centrifugal force, which not only preserves them in their orbits, but prevents them from rushing all together, by the common universal law of gravity, &#8230; "</li>\n<li><a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=80VZAAAAcAAJ&amp;pg=PA48" rel="nofollow">On page 48</a>, Wright stated that the form of the Milky Way is a ring:  " &#8230; the stars are not infinitely dispersed and distributed in a promiscuous manner throughout all the mundane space, without order or design, &#8230;  this ph&#230;nomenon [is] no other than a certain effect arising from the observer\'s situation, &#8230;  To a spectator placed in an indefinite space, &#8230; it [i.e. the Milky Way (<i>Via Lactea</i>)] [is] a vast ring of stars &#8230; "</li>\n<li><a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=80VZAAAAcAAJ&amp;pg=PA65" rel="nofollow">On page 65</a>, Wright speculated that the central body of the Milky Way, around which the rest of the galaxy revolves, might not be visible to us:  " ... the central body A, being supposed as <i>incognitum</i> [i.e. an unknown], without [i.e. outside of] the finite view; ... "</li>\n<li><a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=80VZAAAAcAAJ&amp;pg=PA73" rel="nofollow">On page 73</a>, Wright called the Milky Way the <i>Vortex Magnus</i> (the great whirlpool) and estimated its diameter to be 8.64&#215;10<sup>12</sup> miles (13.9&#215;10<sup>12</sup> km).</li>\n<li><a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=80VZAAAAcAAJ&amp;pg=PA33" rel="nofollow">On page 33</a>, Wright speculated that there are a vast number of inhabited planets in the galaxy:  " &#8230;&#160;; therefore we may justly suppose, that so many radiant bodies [i.e. stars] were not created barely to enlighten an infinite void, but to &#8230; display an infinite shapeless universe, crowded with myriads of glorious worlds, all variously revolving round them; and &#8230; with an inconceivable variety of beings and states, animate &#8230; "</li></ul>\n</span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-218"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-218">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Immanuel Kant, <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=nCcaAQAAMAAJ&amp;pg=PP9" rel="nofollow"><i>Allgemeine Naturgeschichte und Theorie des Himmels</i> &#8230;</a> [Universal Natural History and Theory of Heaven &#8230; ], (Koenigsberg and Leipzig, (Germany):  Johann Friederich Petersen, 1755).\nOn pages 2&#8211;3, Kant acknowledged his debt to Thomas Wright:  <i>"Dem Herrn Wright von Durham, einen Engel&#228;nder, war es vorbehalten, einen gl&#252;cklichen Schritt zu einer Bemerkung zu thun, welche von ihm selber zu keiner gar zu t&#252;chtigen Absicht gebraucht zu seyn scheinet, und deren n&#252;tzliche Anwendung er nicht genugsam beobachtet hat.  Er betrachtete die Fixsterne nicht als ein ungeordnetes und ohne Absicht zerstreutes Gewimmel, sondern er fand eine systematische Verfassung im Ganzen, und eine allgemeine Beziehung dieser Gestirne gegen einen Hauptplan der Raume, die sie einnehmen."</i>  (To Mr. Wright of Durham, an Englishman, it was reserved to take a happy step towards an observation, which seemed, to him and to no one else, to be needed for a clever idea, the exploitation of which he hasn\'t studied sufficiently.  He regarded the fixed stars not as a disorganized swarm that was scattered without a design; rather, he found a systematic shape in the whole, and a general relation between these stars and the principal plane of the space that they occupy.)</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-219"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-219">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Kant (1755), <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=nCcaAQAAMAAJ&amp;pg=PP49" rel="nofollow">pages xxxiii&#8211;xxxvi of the Preface (Vorrede):</a> <i>"Ich betrachtete die Art neblichter Sterne, deren Herr von Maupertuis in der Abhandlung von der Figur der Gestirne gedenket, und die die Figur von mehr oder weniger offenen Ellipsen vorstellen, und versicherte mich leicht, da&#223; sie nichts anders als eine H&#228;ufung vieler Fixsterne seyn k&#246;nnen.  Die jederzeit abgemessene Rundung dieser Figuren belehrte mich, da&#223; hier ein unbegreiflich zahlreiches Sternenheer, und zwar um einen gemeinschaftlichen Mittelpunkt, m&#252;ste geordnet seyn, weil sonst ihre freye Stellungen gegen einander, wohl irregul&#228;re Gestalten, aber nicht abgemessene Figuren vorstellen w&#252;rden.  Ich sahe auch ein:  da&#223; sie in dem System, darinn sie sich vereinigt befinden, vornemlich auf eine Fl&#228;che beschr&#228;nkt seyn m&#252;&#223;ten, weil sie nicht zirkelrunde, sondern elliptische Figuren abbilden, und da&#223; sie wegen ihres blassen Lichts unbegreiflich weit von uns abstehen."</i>  (I considered the type of nebulous stars, which Mr. de Maupertuis considered in his treatise on the shape of stars, and which present the figures of more or less open ellipses, and I readily assured myself, that they could be nothing else than a cluster of fixed stars.  That these figures always measured round informed me that here an inconceivably numerous host of stars, [which were clustered] around a common center, must be orderly, because otherwise their free positions among each other would probably present irregular forms, not measurable figures.  I also realized:  that in the system in which they find themselves bound, they must be restricted primarily to a plane, because they display not circular, but elliptical figures, and that on account of their faint light, they are located inconceivably far from us.)</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-our_galaxy-220"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-our_galaxy_220-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Evans, J. C. (November 24, 1998). <a class="external text" href="http://physics.gmu.edu/~jevans/astr103/CourseNotes/ECText/ch20_txt.htm" rel="nofollow">"Our Galaxy"</a>. George Mason University. <a class="external text" href="https://archive.is/20120630/http://physics.gmu.edu/~jevans/astr103/CourseNotes/ECText/ch20_txt.htm" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on June 30, 2012<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">January 4,</span> 2007</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Our+Galaxy&amp;rft.pub=George+Mason+University&amp;rft.date=1998-11-24&amp;rft.aulast=Evans&amp;rft.aufirst=J.+C.&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fphysics.gmu.edu%2F~jevans%2Fastr103%2FCourseNotes%2FECText%2Fch20_txt.htm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMilky+Way"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-221"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-221">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The term <i>Weltinsel</i> (island universe) appears nowhere in Kant\'s book of 1755.  The term first appeared in 1850, in the third volume of von Humboldt\'s <i>Kosmos</i>:  Alexander von Humboldt, <i>Kosmos</i>, vol. 3 (Stuttgart &amp; T&#252;bingen, (Germany):  J.G. Cotta, 1850), pages 187, 189.  <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=Su0iAQAAMAAJ&amp;pg=PA187" rel="nofollow">From page 187:</a> <i>"Thomas Wright von Durham, Kant, Lambert und zuerst auch William Herschel waren geneigt die Gestalt der Milchstra&#223;e und die scheinbare Anh&#228;ufung der Sterne in derselben als eine Folge der abgeplatteten Gestalt und ungleichen Dimensionen der </i>Weltinsel<i> (Sternschict) zu betrachten, in welche unser Sonnensystem eingeschlossen ist."</i>  (Thomas Wright of Durham, Kant, Lambert and first of all also William Herschel were inclined to regard the shape of the Milky Way and the apparent clustering of stars in it as a consequence of the oblate shape and unequal dimensions of the <i>world island</i> (star stratum), in which our solar system is included.)<br/>\nIn the English translation&#8212;Alexander von Humboldt with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E.C._Ott%C3%A9" title="E.C. Ott&#233;">E.C. Ott&#233;</a>, trans., <i>Cosmos</i> &#8230; (New York City:  Harper &amp; Brothers, 1897), vols. 3&#8211;5&#8212;see <a class="external text" href="http://babel.hathitrust.org/cgi/pt?id=njp.32101057350447;view=1up;seq=157" rel="nofollow">page 147</a>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-222"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-222">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">William Herschel (1785) "On the Construction of the Heavens," <i>Philosophical Transactions of the Royal Society of London</i>, <b>75</b>&#160;: 213&#8211;266.  Herschel\'s diagram of the Milky Way appears immediately after the article\'s last page.
